Sixteenth Argument: Hadith of Hisham al-Dastuwani
Mu'tazila cite a hadith where God created people pure and devils misled them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use this hadith to argue that God does not directly lead people astray, but devils do.

Core Idea
God created humans pure; devils, not God, lead them astray.

The Mu'tazila often appeal to scripture to support their view that humans are created with a sound nature and that external factors cause misguidance. This hadith, narrated by Hisham al-Dastuwani, is a key text. As you read it, notice how the Prophet explicitly states that God created people as hunafa' (pure monotheists) and that devils turned them away. The Mu'tazila then draw the conclusion that anyone who claims God leads astray is contradicting this hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Pay attention to the key phrases: 'خلقت عبادي حنفاء' (I created My servants as hunafa') and 'أتاهم الشيطان فاحتالهم عن دينهم' (the devils came to them and turned them away from their religion).
The hadith is part of a longer sermon, but the Mu'tazila focus on these specific lines.
2
Identify the core claim about creation
The hadith states that God created all people as hunafa'—meaning inclined to pure monotheism and sound nature. This implies an original state of goodness and guidance.
The term 'حنفاء' is plural of 'حنيف', which denotes one who turns away from falsehood towards truth.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite inference
The Mu'tazila argue that since God created people pure, misguidance must come from elsewhere—namely, the devils. Therefore, God does not create disbelief or evil in humans directly.
This supports their doctrine of divine justice, where God does not cause evil.
4
Reflect on the accusation
The passage concludes that whoever says God leads people astray is accusing the Prophet of lying. This shows how the Mu'tazila use this hadith to refute predestinarian views.
This is a strong rhetorical move—charging opponents with contradicting a clear prophetic statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة عشر : ما روى هشام الدستواني قال : حدثنا قتادة عن مطرف بن عبد الله بن الشخير عن عياض بن حماد أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال ذات يوم في خطبته ( إلا إن ربي أمرني أن أعلمكم ما جهلتم ، مما علمني في يومي هذا : كل مال نحلت عبادي فهو حلال لهم، وإني خلقت عبادي حنفاء كلهم . وإنهم أتاهم الشيطان فاحتالهم عن دينهم ، وحرم عليهم ما حللت لهم وهذه الخطبة طويلة . والمقصود منها : أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام صرح بأنه تعالى خلق الخلق حنفاء على السلامة الأصلية وإن الشياطين هم الذين أضلوهم عن الحق . فمن قال : إن الله هو أصلهم عن الدين ، فقد جاهر بتكذيب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في هذه الخطبة
The sixteenth argument: What Hisham al-Dastuwani narrated: He said: Qatadah narrated to us from Mutarrif ibn 'Abdillah ibn al-Shikhkhir from 'Iyad ibn Himmar that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said one day in his sermon: 'Indeed my Lord commanded me to teach you what you are ignorant of, from what He taught me on this day of mine: every wealth I have given to My servants is lawful for them, and I created My servants all as hunafa' (pure monotheists). Then the devils came to them and turned them away from their religion, and made unlawful for them what I had made lawful for them.' This sermon is long. The intended point from it is that he عليه الصلاة والسلام explicitly stated that Allah, exalted, created the creation as hunafa' upon original soundness, and that the devils are the ones who led them astray from the truth. So whoever says that Allah is the one who led them astray from the religion has openly accused the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم of lying in this sermon.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the sixteenth argument of the Mu'tazila, based on a hadith narrated by Hisham al-Dastuwani. The hadith reports that the Prophet said in a sermon that God created all His servants as hunafa' (inclined to pure monotheism and sound nature). Then the devils came and turned them away from their religion. The Mu'tazila argue that this explicitly shows that God created humans on an original sound nature ('ala al-salamah al-asliyyah), and it is the devils, not God, who lead them astray. Therefore, anyone who claims that God Himself leads people astray is contradicting this hadith and accusing the Prophet of falsehood. This supports the Mu'tazilite position that human misguidance comes from external factors (like devils) and not from God's direct creation of evil or disbelief in humans.

Key Terms
حنفاء
الشيطان
أصل/أضل
السلامة الأصلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God creates disbelief directly in humans.
Correction: This hadith attributes misguidance to devils, not God, implying God creates humans with a sound nature.

Checkpoint
What is the main point the Mu'tazila derive from the hadith of Hisham al-Dastuwani?
A) God creates all humans as disbelievers.
B) God created humans pure, and devils are the ones who lead them astray.
C) Humans are born sinful.
D) Devils are created by humans.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use this hadith to argue that God created humans upon a sound nature, and misguidance comes from devils, not from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This hadith says God created you as a hunaf (pure). When you look at your current state—your doubts, your sins, your way from what is pure—can you honestly say your wandering is only from external devils, or do you also recognize your own choices? What will you do today to return to that original purity?
?
The Mu'tazila used this hadith to argue that God does not lead astray. But if you believe God is in control of all things, how do you reconcile that with the devils' role? Does your belief lead you to blame others for your sins, or do you take full responsibility before God?
?
If you truly believed that God created you with a pure nature, how would that change the way you view yourself and your potential for change? What is one area in your life where you have accepted misguidance as your fixed state, and what would it take to reclaim your fitrah?

Seventeenth Argument: Hadith of Abu Huraira
Mu'tazila use a hadith commanding piety and contentment to argue against divine creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use a Prophetic command to argue that humans are the true agents of their actions, not God.

Core Idea
Commands only make sense if the listener can choose to obey.

The Mu'tazila often use scriptural evidence to support their view on free will. In this argument, they cite a well-known hadith where the Prophet commands Abu Huraira to adopt certain virtues. For them, the very presence of a sincere command proves that humans have the ability to perform or refrain from those actions, which would be impossible if God created all actions directly.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Start by reading the Arabic hadith and its English translation. Notice the four specific commands given by the Prophet.
The commands are: piety, contentment, loving for others, and good neighborliness.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite inference
After the hadith, the author states the point of inference: the Prophet's encouragement (hathth wa targhib) would be invalid if the actions were created by God. Ask yourself why that is.
Encouragement implies the person has a choice; otherwise it's meaningless.
3
Connect to free will
Reflect on how this argument supports the Mu'tazilite position that humans create their own actions, against the Ash'ari view of divine creation.

Source Text

الحجة السابعة عشر : ما روى أبو هريرة رضي الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : يا أبا هريرة كن ورعا تكن أعبد الناس ، وكن قنعاً تكن أشكر الناس ، وأحب للناس ما تحب لنفسك تكن مؤمناً ، وأحسن مجاورة من جاورك تكن مسلماً، ووجه الاستدلال بهذا الخبر : أنه حثه على الإتيان بهذه الأفعال ، فلو كانت من خلق الله ، لصار هذا الحث والترغيب باطلا
The seventeenth argument: What Abu Huraira رضي الله عنه narrated: He said: The Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم said: 'O Abu Huraira, be pious, you will be the most devout of people; be content, you will be the most grateful of people; love for people what you love for yourself, you will be a believer; and be a good neighbor to your neighbor, you will be a Muslim.' The point of inference from this report is that he encouraged him to perform these actions. So if they were from Allah's creation, this encouragement and persuasion would be invalid.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the seventeenth Mu'tazilite argument, based on a hadith where the Prophet commands Abu Huraira to do specific good deeds: piety, contentment, loving for others what one loves for oneself, and good neighborliness. The Mu'tazila argue that the very act of command, encouragement, and persuasion (hathth wa targhib) implies that the person has the ability to choose to perform these actions. If these actions were directly created by God (i.e., if they were acts of God and not of the human), then the command would be meaningless, because the person would have no agency to comply or disobey. Therefore, the hadith proves that humans are the true agents of their own actions, supporting the Mu'tazilite view that God does not create human actions.

Key Terms

قناعة
حث
ترغيب
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that commands are merely informative statements about what is good, not requiring any ability to obey.
Correction: The Mu'tazila insist that genuine commands presuppose the listener's ability to choose to obey or disobey; otherwise, the command would be pointless.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila consider the hadith's commands as proof against divine creation of actions?
A) Because the commands are impossible to fulfill.
B) Because commands and encouragement lose their meaning if God creates both the action and the response.
C) Because the hadith is weak.
D) Because the Prophet did not mean what he said.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's commands to Abu Huraira prove that humans have the ability to choose their actions, otherwise the encouragement would be pointless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this hadith, do you feel the weight of personal responsibility, or do you unconsciously deflect it onto qadar? What would change if you truly believed you could choose to be pious right now?
?

?
If your actions are genuinely your own, then the rewards the Prophet promises are within your reach. Which of the four commands—piety, contentment, love for others, or good neighborliness—do you find hardest to own today? What is one concrete step you will take within the next hour to obey it?

Mu'tazilite Argument from Hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar
Mu'tazila cite a hadith to argue for human moral responsibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar to argue that humans have control over their faith and are morally responsible for their beliefs.

Core Idea
The command to die as a believer implies humans can choose faith.

The Mu'tazila present their eighteenth argument, based on a short hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar. In this hadith, the Prophet ﷺ commands those who wish to be saved from Hell to die as believers. The Mu'tazila see this command as evidence that faith is within human control, for otherwise the command would be meaningless. This lesson examines the text and its theological implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic hadith and its English translation. Notice the imperative structure: 'let his death overtake him while he is a believer.'
2
Understand the command
The hadith addresses those who desire salvation. The command implies that it is within their power to ensure they die as believers.
The word 'let' (فَلْ) is a command particle.
3
Identify the Mu'tazilite inference
The Mu'tazila argue that if faith were created directly by God without human choice, the command would be pointless. Therefore, humans must have free will to maintain faith.
This inference is the core of the argument.
4
Consider the alternative view
Contrast with the position that faith is solely a divine gift. How does that interpretation affect the meaning of the hadith?
5
Summarize the argument
Write a one-sentence summary of the Mu'tazilite argument from this hadith.
This helps consolidate understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة عشر : عن عبد الله بن عمر قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : من أحب أن يزحزح عن النار ، ويدخل الجنة فلتدركه منيته ، وهو مؤمن بالله
The eighteenth argument: From 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar, he said: The Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم said: 'Whoever loves to be removed from the Fire and to enter Paradise, let his death overtake him while he is a believer in Allah.'
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the eighteenth Mu'tazilite argument, based on a short hadith from 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar. The hadith states that whoever desires to be saved from Hell and to enter Paradise should ensure that he dies as a believer (mu'min). The Mu'tazila likely interpret this as evidence that faith is a personal acquisition and an action of the human being, not something directly created by God without human involvement. The command to 'let his death overtake him while he is a believer' implies that the person has the ability to maintain faith until death. If faith were created by God, then the person would have no control over it, and the conditional advice would be futile. Thus, this supports the Mu'tazilite position that humans have free will and are responsible for their own faith and actions.

Key Terms
منية
إيمان
نجاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar suggests that humans are commanded to maintain faith, implying they have control over their beliefs.

B) The command to die as a believer suggests that humans have control over their faith.

D) The hadith has no bearing on free will.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the Prophet's command to die as a believer as evidence that humans have control over their faith and moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Nineteenth Proof: Hadith of Bayan and Ghina
The author presents a hadith as the nineteenth proof for human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hadith about speech clarity is used as proof for human agency in Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
A hadith about speech proves human freedom.

The author presents a prophetic hadith as the nineteenth proof. At first glance, the hadith seems to be about eloquence and silence, but the author interprets it as a clear text (nass) supporting the idea that humans have genuine agency in their actions. This lesson unpacks the hadith and its theological significance.

Guided Path

The hadith is short, so read carefully.

3
Understand the author's claim
The author states that this hadith is a 'nass' (clear text) on the point of disagreement. Why would he think so?
This connects to the broader argument about human agency.

This passage presents the nineteenth proof, a hadith from Abu Hurairah. The author cites the Prophet's statement contrasting 'clarity' (al-bayan) from Allah and 'richness' (al-ghina) from Satan. The author then interprets that this hadith is explicit (nass) regarding the disputed issue, presumably affirming that actions attributed to humans are distinct from God's creation, as the hadith implies human agency in truth-telling and speech.

The Hadith on Planting and Tilling
A hadith with Quranic citation is used as evidence for distinguishing divine and human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a prophetic instruction about word choice serves as evidence for the distinction between divine and human agency.

Core Idea
Word choice reveals theological boundaries: only Allah truly plants.

This lesson examines a short but powerful proof from the Prophet's teaching. The hadith commands believers to say 'I tilled' rather than 'I planted,' and cites a Quranic verse that attributes planting to Allah. The author presents this as clear evidence that we must distinguish between Allah's act of creation and the servant's act of cultivation.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and verse
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the shift from 'I planted' to 'I tilled' and the Quranic reference that grounds the command.
The verse is Quran 56:63-64.
2
Understand the distinction
Identify the two actions: 'tilling' (human effort) and 'planting' (divine creative act). Consider why the Prophet insists on this verbal distinction.
The author says this is 'among the clearest evidences.'
3
Reflect on the theological point
Ask yourself: what is at stake if we say 'I planted' without qualification? How does this connect to debates about human free will and divine omnipotence?
The proof supports distinguishing God's act from the servant's act.
4
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz (provided in the lesson) to test recall of the hadith's wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents the twentieth proof, another hadith from Abu Hurairah. The Prophet forbids saying 'I planted' and commands saying 'I tilled,' citing Quranic verse 56:63-64 which attributes planting to Allah. The author concludes that this clearly necessitates distinguishing between the act of Allah (creating the plant) and the act of the servant (tilling the soil). This proof supports the view that human actions have a real human component.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Objection from Abu Bakr and the Ash'ari Response
Ash'ari answer to the objection from Abu Bakr about error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari scholars reconcile the doctrine that Allah creates all actions with Abu Bakr's statement that error is from Satan.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's 'error is from Satan' denies divine approval, not divine creation.

This lesson addresses a key objection against the Ash'ari belief that Allah creates all actions, including errors. Opponents cite a famous report about Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, who said that if his opinion on al-kalalah was correct, it was from Allah, but if it was an error, it was from Satan. How do Ash'ari theologians interpret this without contradicting their creed? They distinguish between Allah's judgment (hukm) and His creative act (takwin). Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note how the objection is presented and then answered.
The passage includes a Quranic verse for support.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors quote Abu Bakr's statement to argue that error is not created by Allah. Find the exact phrase they focus on.
Abu Bakr says error is 'from Satan' and that Allah and His Messenger are innocent of it.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The Ash'ari scholars interpret Abu Bakr's words as referring to Allah's judgment and decree, not to creation. What is the key distinction they make?
4
See the Quranic support
The passage compares Abu Bakr's statement to Quran 3:78, where the hypocrites claim something is 'from Allah' while it is not. How does this parallel strengthen the response?
In both cases, 'from Allah' means by His command/approval, not by His creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: أ - اشتهر عن أبي بكر الصديق، أنه لما سأل عن معنى الكلالة. قال: وأقول فيها برأيي. فإن يك صوابا فمن الله، وإن يك خطأ فمن الشيطان والله ورسوله منه بريئان: وهذا تصريح منه بأن ذلك الخطأ ليس من الله. قال أصحابنا: المراد منه: أن ذلك ليس من الله ومن رسوله، بمعنى الحكم والقضاء به. فأما أن يكون المراد أنه ليس منه بمعنى الخلق والتكوين، فممنوع. وتمام الكلام ههنا، كما في قوله تعالى: (ويقولون هو من عند الله وما هو من عند الله) (١)
They said: A - It is famous about Abu Bakr al-Siddiq that when he was asked about the meaning of al-kalalah, he said: I say about it according to my opinion. If it is correct, it is from Allah, and if it is an error, it is from Satan, and Allah and His Messenger are innocent of it. And this is an explicit statement from him that that error is not from Allah. Our companions said: What is meant [by Abu Bakr] is that it is not from Allah and His Messenger in the sense of judgment and decree. As for the meaning that it is not from Him in the sense of creation and bringing into existence, it is denied. And the complete statement here is like the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And they say it is from Allah, while it is not from Allah' (Quran 3:78).
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the actions of the Companions against the Ash'ari doctrine that all actions, including errors and sins, are created by Allah. The objectors cite the well-known report of Abu Bakr when he gave his opinion on the meaning of al-kalalah. Abu Bakr stated that if his opinion is correct, it is from Allah, but if it is an error, it is from Satan, and Allah and His Messenger are innocent of it. This explicitly states that the error is not from Allah. The Ash'ari response, given by 'our companions' (أصحابنا), clarifies that Abu Bakr meant that the error is not from Allah in terms of judgment and decree (الحكم والقضاء), meaning Allah does not command or approve of error, not that Allah did not create it. The possibility that Abu Bakr meant it in terms of creation and bringing into existence (الخلق والتكوين) is rejected. They support this by drawing a parallel to Quran 3:78, where the hypocrites falsely claim that their words are from Allah, and the verse denies that they are from Allah, understood in the sense of judgment, not creation. Thus, the objection is refuted by distinguishing between Allah's decree and His creation.

Key Terms
الكلالة
رأي
الشيطان
خلق
تكوين
حكم
قضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Abu Bakr's statement means Allah did not create the error, contradicting the Ash'ari view that Allah creates all actions.
Correction: The Ash'aris clarify that Abu Bakr meant Allah does not command or approve error, not that He did not create it.

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'ari scholars interpret Abu Bakr's statement that error is 'not from Allah'?
A) They accept it literally, denying that Allah creates error.
B) They interpret it as referring to judgment and decree, not creation.
C) They reject the report as unreliable.
D) They claim Abu Bakr was mistaken.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response resolves the apparent contradiction by interpreting Abu Bakr's 'not from Allah' as referring to divine approval, not divine creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a mistake—especially in matters of religion—do you immediately attribute the action to Satan or your own weakness, while forgetting that Allah is the Creator of that very action? How does the Ash'ari distinction between divine creation and divine approval challenge you to take responsibility for your errors while still affirming Allah's absolute sovereignty? Write down one specific mistake you made recently and consider: Was it created by Allah? Was it approved by Him? What does that mean for your repentance?
?
Abu Bakr, the greatest of the Companions, spoke with humility about his own opinion. When you assert a religious view, do you leave room for the possibility that you might be wrong, and that the error could come from your own nafs or Shaytan? Or do you speak as if your understanding is infallible? Commit to reciting the dua of humility (Rabbi zidni 'ilma) before giving any fatwa or strong opinion.
?
The objectors in this lesson used a revered figure's words to support a theological position. Have you ever taken a quote from a scholar or a text and used it in a way that ignored the context or deeper meaning? How can you train yourself to seek the intended meaning behind statements, especially when they seem to contradict established truths of the faith? Identify one text you may have misunderstood and resolve to study it with a teacher or reliable commentary.

The Objection from Umar's Correction
Ash'ari explanation of Umar's correction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Umar changed the phrase 'This is what Allah showed Umar' and how the Ash'aris defend divine causality from this incident.

Core Idea
Umar's change was about precision, not denial of divine causality.

The incident of Umar correcting his scribe's phrase is often cited by opponents of the Ash'ari position. They claim it proves that Umar rejected attributing his opinion to Allah's creation. However, Ash'ari scholars offer a nuanced explanation. This lesson examines their response and the principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative
Read the passage about Umar's correction carefully. Note the exact wording change and the companions' commentary.
The Arabic uses 'أرى الله' (showed) versus 'رأى' (saw).
2
Identify the objection
Understand why this incident is used as an argument against the Ash'ari view. Opponents claim Umar denied that his opinion was from Allah.
3
Learn the Ash'ari response
See how the companions explained Umar's action: claiming 'Allah showed me' is only truthful when based on an explicit text (nass). Otherwise, one should say 'I saw' (i.e., my opinion).
This preserves the doctrine that all things are from Allah, but restricts explicit attribution to revelation.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on how this upholds divine causality while maintaining precision in speech. Consider why this distinction matters in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - وأما عمر بن الخطاب فيروى أن كاتبه كتب: وهذا ما أرى الله عمر: فقال للكاتب: امحه. فمحاه. فقال: اكتب هذا ما رأى عمر. قال الأصحاب: قوله: (هذا ما أرى الله عمر) لا يصدق إلا فيما عرف بنص. ولهذا السبب غيره.
B - As for Umar ibn al-Khattab, it is narrated that his scribe wrote: 'This is what Allah showed Umar.' So he said to the scribe: 'Erase it.' So he erased it. Then he said: 'Write: This is what Umar saw.' The companions said: His statement 'This is what Allah showed Umar' is only truthful in matters known by a text. And for this reason he changed it.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents another objection based on the action of Umar. The report states that when Umar's scribe wrote 'This is what Allah showed Umar' at the end of a document, Umar ordered him to erase it and replace it with 'This is what Umar saw.' The objectors argue that Umar denied that his opinion was shown by Allah, implying that opinions are not from Allah's creation or guidance. The Ash'ari scholars explain that Umar's statement 'This is what Allah showed Umar' is only truthful when the matter is known by an explicit text (نص) from revelation. In the absence of such a text, one cannot claim that Allah showed him, because that would be asserting certainty without warrant. Therefore, Umar changed it to 'This is what Umar saw' to be precise, not because he denied divine causality. This preserves the doctrine that all things are from Allah, but restricted to appropriate contexts.

Key Terms
أرى الله
نص
رأي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Umar's correction proves that he denied divine causality for human opinions.
Correction: Umar only changed the phrase because such a claim requires an explicit text (nass); he did not deny that Allah is the ultimate creator of all actions.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari scholars, why did Umar amend the scribe's phrase 'This is what Allah showed Umar'?
A) Because Umar denied that Allah shows anything to humans.
B) Because such a statement is only true when based on an explicit text (nass).
C) Because Umar was correcting a grammatical error.
D) Because Umar wanted to assert his own independent reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Umar's correction teaches that claiming divine inspiration requires explicit textual basis, not that all actions are independent of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever claimed that a decision or opinion you held was from Allah without a clear textual basis? What evidence would you need to honestly say 'Allah showed me'?
?
Umar changed a phrase for the sake of precision. Are you careful with your words when speaking about Allah's guidance? What phrase do you need to correct today?
?
The companions emphasized that 'This is what Allah showed' is only truthful with explicit text. In what area of your life are you most tempted to assert divine guidance without such evidence? How will you humble yourself before the text?

The Objection from Umar's Treatment of the Thief
Objection from Umar's punishment of a thief invoking divine decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of an objection against Ash'ari qadar doctrine based on Umar's punishment of a thief who blamed divine decree.

Core Idea
Does Umar's flogging for blaming Qadar disprove divine decree?

This lesson presents a specific objection raised against the Ash'ari position that all actions, including sin, are decreed by Allah. The objection is based on a report about Umar's treatment of a thief who excused his theft by citing Allah's decree. The thief received an extra punishment for that excuse, suggesting that his claim was false. Understanding this objection helps clarify the stakes of the qadar debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. This preserves the objection's original formulation before interpretation.
2
Identify the objection's logic
Notice that Umar imposes two punishments: hand-cutting for theft and flogging for false attribution. The objectors argue that the flogging shows the thief's claim of divine decree was false, implying that sins are not decreed.
The objection treats the extra punishment as a refutation of the thief's qadar-based excuse.
3
Consider why this challenges Ash'aris
Ash'aris hold that all actions occur by Allah's decree, including sins. If Umar punished the thief for claiming divine decree, it seems to contradict this position. Think about how an Ash'ari might respond.
The actual Ash'ari response is not in this passage, but the objection itself is the focus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - قالوا: ونقل عنه أنه لما أخذ له سارق، وقال له: لم سرقت؟ قال له: بقضاء الله. قطع يده وجلده (٢) وقال: هذا لسرقتك، وهذا لافترائك.
C - They said: And it is narrated from him that when a thief was brought to him, and he said to him: 'Why did you steal?' He replied: 'By the decree of Allah.' So he cut off his hand and flogged him (2) and said: 'This is for your theft, and this is for your false attribution.'
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection based on a report about Umar. When Umar apprehended a thief and asked why he stole, the thief excused himself by saying it was by the decree of Allah (بقضاء الله). Umar responded by both cutting off his hand (the prescribed punishment for theft) and additionally flogging him, saying the flogging was for the false attribution (لافترائك) – meaning the thief's claim that the theft was due to Allah's decree. The objectors argue that Umar's additional punishment for using Qadar as an excuse shows that the thief was lying, implying that sins are not decreed by Allah. This would contradict the Ash'ari position that all actions, including sins, occur by Allah's decree. However, the Ash'ari answer is not provided in the text of this page. This lesson presents only the objection; the response is expected elsewhere in the work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Umar's double punishment disproves the doctrine of divine decree for sins.
Correction: Ash'aris would distinguish between decree (qada) and blameworthiness, arguing that while Allah decrees all actions, humans are still responsible and punishable for their choices. Umar's extra punishment targets the false attribution of sin to Allah, not the decree itself.

Checkpoint

A) Because the thief stole more than once.
B) Because the thief falsely attributed his theft to Allah's decree.
C) Because Umar was in a bad mood.
D) Because flogging is always added to hand-cutting.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses Umar's extra punishment to argue that attributing theft to divine decree is a lie, challenging the idea that sins are decreed by Allah.

?
Umar distinguished between the theft itself and the false attribution of it to Allah. Is there an area in your life where you have been hiding behind divine decree to avoid confronting your own responsibility? What step can you take today to face that honestly?
?
If you truly believe that every action happens by Allah's decree, how do you reconcile that with the justice of punishment—especially your own accountability? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of that balance?

Ali's Refutation of Fatalism on Qada and Qadar
Ali refutes fatalism and affirms human responsibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali ibn Abi Talib refutes the fatalistic interpretation of qada and qadar by affirming divine decree while preserving human responsibility and the validity of reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Qada and Qadar do not cancel reward and punishment.

In this incident from after the Battle of Siffin, a man asks Ali whether their campaign was by God's decree. Ali affirms that every descent and ascent is by God's decree, but when the man despairs of reward, Ali corrects a dangerous misunderstanding: qada and qadar do not mean compulsion. Instead, they are God's command and will, consistent with human choice and accountability. This lesson explores Ali's argument and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the sequence: the man's question, Ali's first answer, the man's despair, Ali's correction and refutation, the man's satisfaction.
The story has a clear dramatic arc.
2
Identify the fatalistic assumption
The old man initially thinks that if everything is by God's decree, then his efforts have no merit. Pinpoint this assumption in the text.
This is a common misunderstanding.
3
Analyze Ali's refutation
Ali explains that if qada were inevitable (lazim) and qadar determined (hatman), reward and punishment would be invalid. He calls such a view the saying of devils and idolaters.
Focus on the phrase 'قضاء لازماً وقدراً حتماً'.
4
Understand Ali's positive statement
Ali then states that God commanded by choice (takhyir), forbade as a warning (tahdhir), and obligated with ease (taysir). This shows human capacity and responsibility.
Note the three key words: تخيير, تحذير, تيسير.
5
Connect the conclusion
The old man is satisfied when Ali explains that 'qada' here means God's command and will, as in the Quranic verse. Reflect on how this resolves the tension.
Ali quotes 17:23 to illustrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - وأيضاً : نقل عن علي بن أبي طالب أنه لما انصرف من صفين قام إليه شيخ من أهل الحجاز ، فقال : يا أمير المؤمنين أخبرنا عن مسيرنا إلى الشام ، أكان بقضاء الله وقدره ؟ فقال : ( والذي خلق الحبة وبرأ النسمة ، ما هبطنا وادياً ، وما علونا تلعة إلا بقضاء الله وقدره ) فقال الشيخ : عند الله احتسب عنائي ومسيري، والله ما احتسب لي من الأجر شيئا . فقال أمير المؤمنين : لعلك تظن قضاء لازماً ، وقدراً حتماً . لو كان كذلك لبطل الثواب والعقاب ، وسقط الوعد والوعيد ، وما كان يأتي من الله لائحة لمذنب ، ولا محمدة لمحسن . ولا كان المحسن أولى بثواب الإحسان من المذنب ، ولا المذنب كان أولى بالعقوبة من المحسن . تلك مقالة إخوان الشياطين ، وعبدة الأوثان ، وخصماء الرحمن ، وشهود الزور ، وأهل العمي والفجور . تلك قدرية هذه الأمة ومجوسها . إن الله أمر تخييراً ، ونهى تحذيراً وكلف تيسيراً ، ولم يعص مغلوباً ، ولم يطع مكروهاً ، ولا بعث الأنبياء عبثاً ، ولا أرى عجائب الآيات باطلا و ذلك ظن الذين كفروا فويل للذين كفروا من النار . فقال الشيخ : وما ذلك القضاء الذي ساقنا ؟ فقال أمير المؤمنين : أمر الله بذلك وإرادته . ثم تلا عليهم : ( وقضى ربك أن لا تعبدوا إلا إياه ) قال : فنهض الشيخ مسروراً ، وهو يقول : أنت الإمام الذي نرجو بطاعته يوم النشور من الرحمن غفرانا أوضحت من ديننا ما كان مشتبهاً جزاك ربك عنا فيه إحساناً
And also: It is narrated from Ali ibn Abi Talib that when he returned from Siffin, an old man from the people of Hijaz stood before him and said: 'O Commander of the Faithful, inform us about our march to Syria: was it by God's decree and predestination?' He said: 'By Him who created the seed and originated the soul, we did not descend into any valley nor ascend any hill except by God's decree and predestination.' The old man said: 'Before God, I count my exertion and my journey [as wasted], and by God, I do not count any reward for me from this.' The Commander of the Faithful said: 'Perhaps you think it is an inevitable decree and a determined destiny. If that were the case, then reward and punishment would be invalid, promise and threat would fall away, and there would come from God no rebuke for a sinner nor praise for a good-doer. Nor would the good-doer be more deserving of the reward of goodness than the sinner, nor would the sinner be more deserving of punishment than the good-doer. That is the saying of the brothers of devils, the worshippers of idols, the adversaries of the Merciful, the false witnesses, the people of blindness and immorality. Those are the Qadariyya of this nation and its Magians. Indeed, God commanded by choice, forbade as a warning, and obligated with ease. He was not disobeyed overcome, nor obeyed unwillingly. He did not send prophets in vain, nor did He show wondrous signs for no purpose. And that is the assumption of those who disbelieve, so woe to those who disbelieve from the Fire.' Then the old man said: 'And what is that decree that drove us?' The Commander of the Faithful said: 'It is God's command and will regarding that.' Then he recited to them: 'And your Lord has decreed that you worship none but Him.' (17:23) He said: Then the old man stood up happy, saying: 'You are the Imam through whose obedience we hope for forgiveness from the Merciful on the Day of Resurrection. You have clarified what was ambiguous in our religion. May your Lord reward you well.'
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a longer argument from Ali ibn Abi Talib refuting a fatalistic understanding of divine decree. The setting is after the Battle of Siffin, when a man asks Ali whether their campaign was by God's decree. Ali first affirms that indeed everything happens by God's decree. The man then despairs, thinking his efforts are worthless if predetermined. Ali corrects this misunderstanding: he says the man is thinking of qada as inevitable and qadar as determined, which would nullify reward and punishment. Ali then makes a strong statement: such a view is the saying of devils, idolators, etc., and likens them to the Qadariyya and Magians. He then positively states that God's command is one of choice (takhyir), warning (tahdhir), and easy obligation (taysir). God is not disobeyed by force nor obeyed unwillingly. Prophethood is not in vain. He quotes the Quranic verse about God's decree to worship Him alone, showing that 'qada' here means command, not compulsion. The old man is satisfied. The passage thus defends human responsibility alongside divine decree.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If everything is by God's decree (qada and qadar), then human actions are compelled and there is no reward or punishment.
Correction: Ali explains that qada and qadar do not mean compulsion; rather, God's decree is His command and will, which leave room for human choice and accountability. Otherwise, reward and punishment would be meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to Ali, what would happen if qada were 'inevitable' (lazim) and qadar 'determined' (hatman)?
A) Reward and punishment would still be valid.
B) Promise and threat would be strengthened.
C) Reward and punishment would be invalid.
D) Human beings would have no choice at all.

Key Takeaway
Ali teaches that belief in qada and qadar must be understood as God's command and will, not as compulsion that negates human accountability and the validity of reward and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you genuinely believed that every choice you make is recorded and will be rewarded or punished—and that you have full agency—would your daily habits change? Which one habit would you commit to reforming starting today?
?
Ali says that if qada were inevitable, then there would be no distinction between the good-doer and the sinner. Look at your own life: are you living as if your actions matter for your eternal standing? Or do you sometimes act as if God's decree makes your efforts pointless? What needs to change?

Ibn Abbas on the Dangers of Extremes in Qadar
Ibn Abbas warns against both fatalism and denying God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the balanced position of Ibn Abbas on qadar, rejecting both fatalism (jabr) and denial of God's foreknowledge.

Core Idea
Qadar is a deep ocean – stop at its shore.

Ibn Abbas, the great companion and cousin of the Prophet ﷺ, offered timeless guidance on the sensitive topic of qadar. In two brief yet profound statements, he warns against two opposite errors: falsely attributing injustice to God and denying His knowledge. This lesson unpacks his advice, showing how to tread the middle path of piety.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the words of Ibn Abbas come through before you interpret.
Notice that there are two distinct narrations, marked by 'waw' and 'zay' in the Arabic.
2
Identify the two warnings
Ibn Abbas gives two explicit prohibitions: do not say that God compels people to sin, and do not say that God did not know what people would do. Find these in the text and note why each is wrong.
The first wrongs God (tuzlimuhu), the second makes one ignorant of God (tajhalūhu).
3
Understand the balanced stance
After the warnings, Ibn Abbas presents the position of a person with piety: if God punishes, it is for sin; if He pardons, it is by grace. Reflect on how this avoids both extremes.
This affirms both God's justice and His mercy without falling into compulsion or denial of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و - روى مجاهد أن ابن عباس كتب إلى قرى أهل الشام : رسالة طويلة ذكر فيها أنه قال : « هل منكم إلا مفتري على الله ، يحل ما حرمه عليه ، وينسبها علانية إليه )» ز - وعن عكرمة عن ابن عباس أنه قال : القدر بحر عميق ، فقفوا عند أدناه . ولا تقولوا : إنه جبر العباد على المعاصي فتظلموه ، ولا تقولوا : إن الله لم يعلم ما العباد عاملون فتجهلوه . ولكن رجل امتحن الله قلبه للتقوى قال : إن عذب فبذنب ، وإن عفا فبفضل .
W - Mujahid narrated that Ibn Abbas wrote to the villages of the people of Syria a long letter in which he said: 'Is there anyone among you except a slanderer against God, making lawful what He has forbidden, and attributing it openly to Him?' Z - And from Ikrimah from Ibn Abbas that he said: 'Qadar is a deep ocean, so stop at its shore. And do not say that He compelled the servants to sin, for then you would wrong Him. And do not say that God did not know what the servants were doing, for then you would be ignorant of Him. Rather, a man whose heart God has tested for piety says: If He punishes, it is for a sin; and if He pardons, it is by grace.'
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents two statements from Ibn Abbas (radiyallahu 'anhuma). The first, from a letter to Syria, condemns those who make lawful what God has forbidden and then openly attribute that to God – i.e., falsely ascribing their own rulings to God. The second statement is a famous advice about qadar: it is a deep ocean, so do not go beyond its shore. Ibn Abbas warns against two extremes: saying that God compels people to sin (which would wrong God by attributing injustice to Him) and saying that God does not know what people will do (which is ignorance of God). Instead, the proper stance is that of a person with piety: if God punishes, it is due to sin; if He pardons, it is by grace. This affirms both God's justice and His foreknowledge without falling into compulsion or denial of knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibn Abbas completely forbids discussing qadar.
Correction: Ibn Abbas forbids going to extremes (saying God compels or does not know), but he does not forbid all discussion; he himself presents a balanced view.

Checkpoint

B) That He knows everything
C) That He is just

Key Takeaway
Ibn Abbas teaches that qadar is a profound matter; one must avoid both fatalism and denial of divine foreknowledge, instead affirming that punishment is for sin and pardon is by grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Did you immediately search your own actions for a sin, or did you assume it was just 'fate'? How can you cultivate the heart of a man 'tested for piety' who readily acknowledges his own fault?

Ali ibn Abdullah on the Punishment for Fatalists
Ali ibn Abdullah threatens to kill anyone who claims God compels sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the severe condemnation of jabr (divine compulsion of sin) by the early Salaf, as exemplified by the response of Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
Ibn Abbas threatened to kill anyone who claimed God compels sin.

This lesson presents a powerful narration from the family of Ibn Abbas. It shows the uncompromising stance of the Companions and their students against the doctrine that God forces human beings to sin. The harsh reaction of Ibn Abbas underscores the gravity of attributing injustice to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the narration
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Notice the context: Ali is sitting with his father, and a man reports a claim.
2
Identify the heresy
The man reports that some people claim their actions come from God and that God compelled them to sin. This is the doctrine of jabr.
3
Understand Ibn Abbas's response
Ibn Abbas says he would seize such a person by the throat and throw him down until he dies. This is not a metaphor; it is a literal threat showing the seriousness of the error.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Why such a harsh reaction? Because jabr implies God is unjust and contradicts human accountability. The Salaf defended divine justice fiercely.

Source Text

A man informs him that there are people who claim that God compelled them to sin. Ibn Abbas responds with a very severe threat: if he knew any such person was present, he would physically attack them and kill them. This shows the strong rejection of the doctrine of jabr (compulsion) among the early Salaf. It demonstrates that the belief that God forces people to sin is considered extremely dangerous and warrants harsh punishment. The passage implies that such a belief leads to slandering God's justice.

Zayn al-Abidin's Threefold Classification of Human Actions
Zayn al-Abidin uses a logical division to refute both fatalism and freewill.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Zayn al-Abidin's logical division of human actions and how it refutes both fatalism and complete freewill by showing that blame and punishment logically require human agency.

Core Idea
Either actions are from God, from man, or from both—only the third is logically consistent with blame.

Zayn al-Abidin, the grandson of the Prophet, was asked about the nature of human actions. In his response, he presented a simple but powerful logical classification that cuts through the debate on predestination and free will. By exploring the three possibilities and their consequences, he shows why the correct Islamic position lies in affirming human responsibility without denying divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Notice the logical structure: classification followed by implications.
Pay attention to the three possibilities mentioned.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage presents three possibilities for human actions. List them: (1) entirely from God, (2) between God and servant, (3) entirely from servant.
3
Analyze the implications
For each possibility, note what would follow regarding blame and punishment. The passage states the consequence for each.
The consequence for possibility 2 is problematic because it would blame both God and servant.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since blame and punishment are established in Islam, the only viable option is that actions are from the servant. This supports the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb).
The phrase 'that is what we say' indicates the speaker's school position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ط - ونقل أن زين العابدين سأل عن أفعال العباد . فقال : إنها لا تخلو إما أن تكون فعلا الله تعالى ولا صنع للعبد فيه ، أو تكون فعلاً للعبد ولا صنع الله فيه ، أو تكون بينهما . فإن كانت فعلا الله لزم سقوط الذم والعقاب من العبد ، وإن كانت بينهما فاللوم عليهما ، وإن كانت من العبد فهو الذي نقوله
T - And it is narrated that Zayn al-Abidin was asked about the actions of the servants. He said: They are not free from being either an act of God Almighty with no making (action) of the servant in it, or an act of the servant with no act of God in it, or between them. If it is an act of God, then blame and punishment from the servant necessarily fall away. If it is between them, then blame is on both. And if it is from the servant, that is what we say.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents a logical classification by Zayn al-Abidin (Ali ibn al-Husayn) regarding the actions of human beings. He divides the possibilities into three: either the action is entirely from God with no human contribution, or entirely from the human with no divine contribution, or a combination of both. He then examines the implications: if actions were entirely from God, then it would be impossible to blame or punish the human. If they were between them, then both would be blameworthy. Since we know from revelation that humans are blamed and punished, the only consistent option is that actions are from the human. This supports the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that humans have kasb (acquisition) of actions, though the text here seems to lean towards human agency. The phrase 'that is what we say' indicates the author's school affirms that actions are from the servant (with divine creation).

Key Terms
أفعال العباد
صنع
ذم
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this passage advocates complete freewill, denying divine involvement entirely.
Correction: The Ash'ari position holds that God creates the action and the human acquires it. The phrase 'from the servant' is compatible with acquisition (kasb), not absolute freewill.

Checkpoint
According to Zayn al-Abidin, if actions were entirely from God, what would follow?
A) Humans would be praised
B) Blame and punishment would be removed
C) Blame would be on both
D) It is not possible

Key Takeaway
Zayn al-Abidin's logical division shows that for blame and punishment to be meaningful, human actions must originate from the human servant (with divine creation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe the action was from you, or do you secretly excuse yourself by thinking God created it and you are innocent? How does Zayn al-Abidin's trilemma force you to take full ownership of your deeds?
?
If you knew that every action of yours is either entirely from God or entirely from you, would you change how you approach repentance? How does this argument make you accountable before you seek forgiveness?
?
Think of a time you were angry at someone for their wrongdoing. According to this passage, if you believe their action was from God, your anger is misplaced. Have you ever wrongly attributed blame to someone for what you considered a divine decree? How will you reconcile this?

Umar's Affirmation of Divine Misguidance
Qadi Abu Bakr narrates Umar's rebuttal to a Christian who denied Allah's misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the early Muslims, including Umar ibn al-Khattab, openly affirmed that Allah is the creator of both guidance and misguidance, contrary to the Mu'tazilite denial.

Core Idea
Umar affirmed: Allah creates guidance and misguidance alike.

This lesson presents a powerful historical narration from Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani. It shows how a Companion of the Prophet, Umar ibn al-Khattab, responded to a Christian leader who denied that Allah can misguide. This episode directly counters the Mu'tazilite claim that early Muslims rejected divine misguidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation side by side. Notice the key terms 'هداية' (guidance) and 'إضلال' (misguidance).
2
Identify the three elements
Identify the three parts: the author's introductory remark, Umar's sermon quote, and his rebuttal to the patriarch. Observe how each part contributes to the doctrinal point.
The rebuttal is the climax: Umar calls the patriarch a liar and affirms Allah's creation of misguidance.
3
Understand the doctrinal significance
Reflect on why this narration is important. It shows that the early Sunni view (that Allah creates all actions, including misguidance) was held by the Companions, contrary to the Mu'tazilite claim.
The patriarch denied that Allah misguides, which was a Christian or Mu'tazilite-influenced view.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question. It focuses on Umar's exact words to the patriarch.
The answer is B: Umar said the patriarch lied and that Allah created and misguided him.

Commentary
This passage presents a narration from Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's book Al-Hidaya. It begins with the author's introductory remark that the Ash'ari scholars (our companions) have transmitted from the early great scholars (the Companions and Salaf) the opposite of what the Mu'tazila claimed. Then follows the narration: During a sermon, Umar ibn al-Khattab recited the well-known verse from the Quran (in meaning) that guidance and misguidance are solely from Allah. A Christian patriarch (jathaliq) objected, understanding that Umar was attributing misguidance to Allah. Umar forcefully rebuked the patriarch, saying he lied, and affirmed that Allah created him, misguides him, and will enter him into Hell. This shows that the early Muslims (the Sahabah) openly affirmed that Allah is the creator of both guidance and misguidance, contrary to the Mu'tazilite denial of divine misguidance.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Umar explicitly said to the patriarch: 'Allah created you, and He misguided you, and He will enter you into the Fire.' This shows that early Muslims affirmed that Allah is the creator of misguidance.

Key Takeaway
Umar's rebuttal proves that the early Muslims unequivocally believed that Allah is the creator of both guidance and misguidance, contrary to later denials.

?

?

Ali's Stance on Qadar: Belief in Good and Bad Decree
Ali declares that disbelief in qadar excludes one from the community.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of belief in qadar as a criterion of belonging to the Muslim community, based on Ali's statement.

Core Idea
Belief in qadar, its good and bad, is a defining mark of the community.

This lesson presents a powerful narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib, delivered from the pulpit of Kufa. In it, Ali makes a strong declaration linking belief in divine decree (qadar) to being part of the Muslim community. This statement underscores that qadar is not a secondary issue but a fundamental article of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the words of Ali sink in.
Notice the chain of narration: al-Sha'bi from Ali.
2
Identify the key phrase
Focus on the phrase 'ليس منا من لم يؤمن بالقدر' (He is not from us who does not believe in qadar). Ask yourself what 'from us' means in this context.
It refers to the community of believers following the Prophet's way.
3
Understand 'good and its bad'
The phrase 'خيره وشره' (its good and its bad) qualifies qadar. Reflect on what these words mean in relation to divine decree.
They refer to events that appear beneficial or harmful in human experience, not moral categories.
4
Connect to the misconception
Some misunderstand 'good and bad' as moral good and evil. Consider why this distinction matters and how the passage refutes it.
5
Reflect on the lesson's importance
Ask yourself: Why did Ali make such a strong statement? How does belief in qadar affect one's identity as a Muslim?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

B - And it is also narrated from al-Sha'bi from Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, that he addressed the people from the pulpit of Kufa. He said: 'He is not from us who does not believe in qadar (divine decree), its good and its bad.'

This passage is a narration from the famous tabi'i al-Sha'bi, quoting Ali ibn Abi Talib. This is a strong statement making belief in qadar a criterion of belonging. It refutes those who deny qadar or distinguish between good and bad in decree, emphasizing that both aspects are from Allah.

Key Terms
القدر
خيره وشره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'good and bad' refers to moral good and evil, implying Allah decrees sin.

Key Takeaway
Ali's statement establishes that belief in qadar, embracing both its pleasant and unpleasant aspects, is essential for being part of the Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Caution cannot avert what Allah has decreed.

In this lesson, we examine a well-known report from Ali ibn Abi Talib (r) before the Battle of Siffin. He recited a poetic verse that, despite textual corruption, conveys a profound truth about qadar: no amount of human caution can change what Allah has written. The commentary of Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani clarifies the lesson.

Ka'b bin Zuhayr: Whatever Allah Decrees Will Happen
Ka'b bin Zuhayr's poetic declaration of the inevitable nature of Allah's decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ka'b bin Zuhayr's poetic line affirms the inevitability of Allah's decree, and that this line was accepted by the Prophet as a correct statement of qadar.

Core Idea
Allah's decree is absolute and inevitably executed.

This lesson examines a line from Ka'b bin Zuhayr's famous poem composed after the conquest of Mecca. In it, he declares that everything Allah has decreed will certainly happen. The Prophet's approval of this poem makes it a significant early testimony to the doctrine of qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The passage is a direct quote from a historical poem.
2

The poem is part of the prophetic tradition.
3

The key phrase is 'fakullu ma qaddara ar-Rahmanu maf'ul' – everything that the Most Merciful has decreed will happen. This is a clear affirmation of the inevitable execution of divine decree.
4
Reflect on the significance for qadar
This line is evidence that the early Muslims, including the Prophet himself, affirmed that Allah's decree is absolute. It counters any notion that qadar can be changed or nullified by human action.
The statement is universal and not limited to personal affairs.

Umar's Justice: Qadar Does Not Excuse Sin
Umar punishes a thief for lying by attributing his sin to qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in divine decree (qadar) does not remove human responsibility, and that using qadar as an excuse for sin is a form of lying against Allah.

Core Idea
Qadar is not a shield for sin; accountability remains.

This brief story from the life of Umar ibn al-Khattab illustrates a critical point about Islamic theology of qadar. When a thief tried to justify his crime by attributing it to Allah's decree, Umar did not accept the excuse. Instead, he imposed an additional punishment for the lie against Allah. This teaches us that qadar does not negate moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the flow: the thief's excuse, Umar's response, and the explanation given.
2
Understand the double punishment
Observe that Umar ordered both lashing and amputation. Identify the reason for each: amputation for theft, lashing for lying about Allah.
3
Extract the theological lesson
Reflect on why Umar considered the thief's statement a lie. What does this tell us about the proper understanding of qadar and human accountability?
This connects qadar to moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأوقع عمر رضي الله عنه عقوبتين على رجل قال هذا القول . فقد جيء إليه بسارق ، فسأله : لم سرقت ؟ فقال : قدر الله ذلك . فقال عمر : اضربوه ثلاثين سوطا ، ثم اقطعوا يده . فقيل : ولم ؟ فقال : يقطع لسرقته ، ويضرب لكذبه على الله .
And Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, inflicted two punishments upon a man who said this statement. For a thief was brought to him, and he asked: 'Why did you steal?' He said: 'Allah decreed that.' So Umar said: 'Strike him thirty lashes, then cut off his hand.' It was asked: 'Why?' He said: 'His hand is cut off for his theft, and he is flogged for his lie against Allah.'
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a story about Umar ibn al-Khattab. A thief was brought before him, and when asked why he stole, the thief replied that Allah had decreed it. Umar did not accept this as an excuse; instead, he ordered a double punishment: lashing for lying about Allah (by attributing the sin to divine decree as a justification) and amputation for the actual theft. The lesson is that qadar is not a licence for sin; humans are still accountable for their actions. Umar's action refutes the Qadariyya (those who deny qadar) but also the fatalists who use qadar to justify wrongdoing.

Key Terms
القدر
الكذب على الله
السرقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that because everything is decreed by Allah, a person is not accountable for their sins.
Correction: This story shows that using qadar as an excuse is a lie against Allah and does not remove punishment.

Checkpoint

B) Because the thief lied against Allah by blaming qadar for his sin.
C) Because the theft was committed in the mosque.
D) Because the thief was a repeated offender.

Key Takeaway
Belief in qadar does not justify sin; humans are still responsible for their choices and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In that moment, were you truly submitting to qadar or avoiding the pain of admitting your own choice? What would it look like to fully own that sin today—without blaming Allah or your circumstances?
?
When you catch yourself making excuses for a repeated sin, do you ever pause to consider that you might be lying about Allah—justifying your action as if He approves or decreed it? What specific excuse are you most tempted to use, and how can you replace it with humble acknowledgment of your fault?
?
Umar valued truth over comfort: he punished lying about Allah more severely than theft. In your own life, where do you prioritize comfort over truth? What is one dishonest belief you hold about yourself, others, or Allah that you need to confront today—even if it hurts?

Labid Affirms Divine Guidance and Misguidance
Labid's poetry asserts that guidance and misguidance are from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Labid's poetry articulates the Sunni doctrine that both guidance and misguidance are from Allah's will.

Core Idea
Guidance and misguidance are entirely in Allah's hands, not our own.

In this passage, Labid ibn Rabi'ah, a pre-Islamic poet who later embraced Islam, expresses a fundamental Islamic belief: that all good is from Allah and that He alone guides or misguides whom He wills. His poetic lines serve as a clear, early affirmation of the Sunni position on divine decree (qadar). As you read, notice how Labid attributes both guidance and misguidance directly to Allah's will, leaving no room for independent human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic poem
Read the Arabic text once to get its rhythmic flow and key terms. Don't worry about full understanding yet.
The poem is concise; pay attention to the words for guidance (هدى) and misguidance (أضل).
2
Compare with the translation
Read the literal translation alongside the Arabic. Notice how each phrase maps to English.
The phrase 'من يهده سبل الخير اهتدى' is central.
3
Identify the theological claim
Labid says 'whom He guides to the paths of good is guided' and 'whom He wills, He misguides.' Identify who 'He' refers to and what this implies about human autonomy.
This directly addresses the Sunni belief about qadar.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this poem refutes the idea that humans can guide themselves or choose guidance independently of Allah.
This is a key point in debates about free will versus predestination.
5
Address the common misunderstanding
The phrase 'whom He wills' might seem to suggest arbitrary action. Understand that in Islamic theology, Allah's will is based on perfect wisdom and justice.
Misunderstanding this can lead to a distorted view of Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - وقال لبيد : إن قربي ربنا خير نفل وبإذن الله ديني والـعـمــل أحمد الله فلاند له بيديه الخير، ما شاء فعل من يهده سبل الخير اهتدى ناعم البال . ومن شاء أضل
E - And Labid said: 'Indeed, closeness to our Lord is a good supererogatory act, and with Allah's permission is my religion and action. I praise Allah, for there is no one except Him. In His hands is goodness; what He wills He does. Whom He guides to the paths of good is guided, at ease of mind. And whom He wills, He misguides.'
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage quotes Labid ibn Rabi'ah, a pre-Islamic poet who became Muslim. In his poem, he acknowledges that all good is in Allah's hands and that Allah does what He wills. The key lines affirm that guidance to the paths of good comes from Allah, and that Allah misguides whom He wills. This is a clear poetic statement of the Sunni position on qadar: both guidance and misguidance are solely from Allah, not from human self-determination.

Key Terms
الهداية
الإضلال
مشيئة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'whom He wills' (man shaa) implies that Allah guides or misguides people arbitrarily, without reason or justice.
Correction: In Sunni theology, Allah's will is always in accordance with His infinite wisdom and justice. He guides those who deserve guidance and misguides those who, in His knowledge, deserve misguidance, but the exact rationale is known only to Him.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Labid's poetry affirms that both guidance and misguidance are solely from Allah's will, reinforcing the Sunni doctrine of qadar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Do Not Imitate the Stubborn Disciple
Author warns against using qadar as an excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that using qadar as an excuse for disobedience is imitating the stubborn disciple, and that true believers obey Allah sincerely and fear His punishment.

Core Idea
Do not use qadar as an excuse; imitate the obedient, not the stubborn.

This passage is a direct exhortation from the author, addressing the readers as 'servants' and warning them against a common misuse of the doctrine of qadar. The author contrasts the fate of the obedient with that of the arrogant who rejected faith. This lesson will help you internalize the practical implication of qadar: that it does not justify sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall message. Note the author's emotional tone and the direct address to the reader.
2
Identify the key warnings
Find the phrases that warn against imitating the stubborn disciple, and the commands to obey Allah and fear His promise and threat.
The author uses imperative verbs to emphasize urgency.
3
Reflect on the description of the arrogant
Consider the description of the one who was arrogant and refused, and what happened to him (left his worldly gains, faced reckoning). This reinforces the consequence of misusing qadar.
This serves as a deterrent example.
4
Connect to the doctrine of qadar
Understand that the author's exhortation is a practical conclusion to earlier proofs about qadar. Qadar does not remove moral responsibility.
The earlier lessons established that qadar is not an excuse for sin.

فيا أيها العبيد لا تتشبهوا بالمريد العنيد ، ولا تقولوا على الحاضر العتيد ، وراقبوا الله في الوعد والوعيد . وأطيعوه في المنهج السديد ، وكونوا على حذر منه في العذاب الشديد . فمن [أتى] بذلك فهو السعيد ، والزمان الذي ينفق فيه ذاك ، فهو المديد تذكروا إذ كنيم نطقاً ثم [الكثيف] يرسب ، واللطيف طفا . وكأن بما بقي وقد انطفأ ، وخير بشدة ما وفى . تذكروا من استكبر وأبي ، وأتى من هذه المذرة كل ما أتى . ثم انتقل إلى دار الآخرة وترك لأعدائه ما حصله من المراتب الفاخرة ، ثم أحضر في موقف الجلال . وعرضت عليه موجبات النكال ، وحوسب بكل مقدار ، عند الملك الجبار .
O servants! do not imitate the stubborn disciple, and do not speak about the Present and Ready [Allah], and fear Allah regarding the promise and the threat. Obey Him in the straight path, and beware of Him regarding the severe punishment. So whoever does that is the happy one, and the time spent in that is extended. Remember when you were speaking, then the thick sinks and the light floats. And as if with what remains, it has extinguished, and good with severity has fulfilled. Remember the one who was arrogant and refused, and did all that he did from this world. Then he moved to the Hereafter and left his enemies what he attained of excellent ranks. Then he was brought to the position of majesty, and the causes of punishment were presented to him, and he was accounted for every measure, before the Compelling King.

Commentary
This passage is a direct exhortation from the author (likely the original writer of the book, perhaps al-Matalib's author). He addresses the readers as 'servants' and warns them not to imitate the stubborn disciple (al-mureed al-'aneed) who uses qadar as an excuse. He instructs them to obey Allah sincerely and fear His punishment. The author then describes the fate of the arrogant who rejected faith: they left their worldly gains behind and faced the reckoning of Allah. The purpose is to prevent misuse of the doctrine of qadar and to encourage righteousness. This serves as a practical conclusion to the earlier proofs about qadar.
