The First Rational Proof for God's Determination of Actions
The author presents the first rational proof for the Ash'ari position on qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the Ash'ari rational proof that the disbeliever's choice of disbelief is determined by God through a preponderating motive.

Core Idea
The disbeliever's choice cannot be explained without a determining factor from God.

This lesson presents the first of several rational proofs for the Ash'ari doctrine of qadar. The proof focuses on the disbeliever who has equal ability to believe or disbelieve, and argues that his choice of disbelief must have a determining factor. If that factor came from the person himself, the argument would regress infinitely, so it must come from God. This is a foundational argument in the debate with the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author contrasts the Ash'ari position with the Mu'tazila's denial. Note the assertion that rational evidence supports the Ash'ari view.
2
Analyze the conditional premise
The proof begins by dividing the disbeliever's situation: either he lacks the ability to believe (the point is moot) or he has equal ability towards both belief and disbelief.
This is a disjunction that covers all possibilities.
3
Follow the argument for a determining factor
If he has equal ability, his choice of disbelief over belief requires a murajjih (determining factor). Without it, the possible would be independent of a cause, which is impossible in kalam.
The impossibility of a possible existing without a cause is a key axiom.
4
Trace the origin of the determining factor
The determining factor cannot come from the servant because that would lead to infinite regress. Therefore it must come from God, who strengthens the motive in the heart, leading to necessity.
Infinite regress is rejected in kalam arguments.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author declares this a definitive proof. Consider why it is considered powerful and how it addresses the Mu'tazila objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن مذهبنا ذلك . وأما المعتزلة فإنهم ينكرون أشد الإنكار . ويدل على صحة قولنا : المعقول والمنقول أما المعقول فوجوه : إما أن البرهان الأول : وهو أن الكافر المقدم على الكفر إن لم يكن متمكناً من الإيمان فقد حصل المطلوب ، وإن كان متمكناً منه كانت قدرته بالنسبة إلى الضدين على السوية . فإقدامه على الكفر دون الإيمان - مع أن ضده كان ممكنات . يستغني عن المرجح أو يفتقر إليه . فإن كان الأول لزم استغناء الممكن عن المرجح . وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني فذلك المرجح ، إن كان من العبد عاد التقسيم فيه ، وإن كان من الله تعالى ، فهو المطلوب. لأنه ظهر أن الكافر ما أقدم على الكفر إلا لأجل أن الله تعالى قوى تلك الداعية في قلبه ، وقد دللنا على أنه متى حصلت الداعية المرجحة ، فإنه لا بد من الوجوب وهذا برهان قاطع لا محيص عنه
Know: that our doctrine is that. As for the Mu'tazila, they strongly deny it. And that which indicates the correctness of our statement is the rational and the transmitted. As for the rational, there are aspects: namely, the first proof: and it is that the disbeliever who proceeds to disbelief, if he is not capable of belief, then the required result is obtained. And if he is capable of it, his power (qudra) with respect to the two opposites is equal. So his proceeding to disbelief rather than belief - even though its opposite was possible - is either independent of the determining factor or in need of it. If the first, then it would necessitate the possible being independent of the determiner, which is impossible. And if the second, then that determining factor: if it is from the servant, the division would repeat regarding it, and if it is from God Almighty, then that is the required result. Because it becomes apparent that the disbeliever only proceeded to disbelief because God Almighty strengthened that motive in his heart. And we have demonstrated that whenever the preponderating motive occurs, then necessity is unavoidable. And this is a definitive proof from which there is no escape.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (al-burhan al-awwal) for the Ash'ari position that God determines human actions, specifically the disbeliever's choice of disbelief. The author begins by stating his school's doctrine and that the Mu'tazila strongly deny it. He then asserts that evidence comes from both rational and transmitted sources, and here he presents the rational evidence. The proof proceeds as a conditional argument: The disbeliever who commits disbelief either has the ability (tamakkun) to believe or not. If he lacks the ability, the point is moot. If he has equal ability towards both belief and disbelief, his power (qudra) is equally related to both opposites. Therefore, his proceeding to disbelief rather than belief, even though belief was possible, requires a determining factor (murajjih) that tips the scale, or else he would be independent of any determiner. If he were independent, that would entail that a possible thing (al-mumkin) can exist without a determiner, which is impossible in kalam (every contingent needs a cause). So he must be in need of a determining factor. That factor either comes from the human servant (al-'abd) or from God. If from the servant, the same question recurs (what determined that determination?), leading to an infinite regress (the division repeats). Therefore, it must come from God. Thus, it becomes evident that the disbeliever only proceeded to disbelief because God strengthened the motive (al-da'iyah) in his heart. The author then states that when the preponderating motive (al-da'iyah al-murajjihah) occurs, the action becomes necessary (wujub). He concludes that this is a definitive proof from which there is no escape.

Key Terms
تمكن
قدرة
ضدين
مرجح
داعية مرجحة
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument proves compulsion (jabr) and negates human responsibility.
Correction: The argument does not deny human power (qudra) but shows that the determining factor comes from God. The human still has equal ability towards both acts; God's determination works through creating the preponderating motive, which makes the action necessary but does not remove human agency entirely in the Ash'ari framework.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what must be the case if the disbeliever is capable of both belief and disbelief but chooses one over the other?
A) His choice is independent of any determining factor.
B) His choice requires a determining factor (murajjih).
C) His choice is purely random.
D) His choice is determined solely by his free will without any external cause.

Key Takeaway
The disbeliever's choice of disbelief over belief, given equal ability, logically requires a determining factor from God, proving that God determines actions through preponderating motives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your every choice—even your disbelief or sin—is ultimately determined by God through preponderating motives, how does that change the way you view your own sins? Will you still make excuses for your disobedience, knowing that you have equal capacity for obedience?
?
The proof shows that the disbeliever's choice is not independent; God strengthens the motive. Reflect on a time you felt a strong inclination to disobey. Did you recognize that motive as something God allowed to be strengthened? What does this teach you about relying on your own willpower alone?
?
Since the preponderating motive makes the action necessary, how urgently will you seek to have God place a preponderating motive toward obedience in your heart? What concrete step will you take today to invite that divine strengthening?

Linguistic Proof that 'Idlaal' Does Not Mean Creating Error
Objection and linguistic proof that 'idlaal' cannot mean creating error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic arguments against interpreting 'yudillu man yashaa' as Allah creating error in the hearts, and why the word 'idlaal' cannot carry that meaning in Arabic.

Core Idea
In Arabic, 'leading astray' does not mean 'creating error'—it means abandoning the person to confusion.

The author begins by presenting an objection: rational evidences show that Allah cannot create disbelief, so the verses must be interpreted. He then provides a detailed linguistic proof that the word 'idlaal' (leading astray) cannot literally mean 'creating error'. This lesson unpacks that linguistic argument, showing how the correct usage of 'idlaal' in Arabic contradicts the theological interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where the author states that rational evidences forbid interpreting the verses as Allah creating disbelief, prompting the need for ta'wil.
2
Examine the linguistic rule
The author explains that in correct Arabic, 'idlaal' is only used when a doubt (shubha) confuses the correct path, not when someone is forced away.
Contrast with the word 'man' (prevent) which is used for compulsion.
3
Consider the cases of Iblis and Pharaoh
Allah calls Iblis and Pharaoh 'mudillun' (leaders astray), but everyone agrees they did not create error in hearts. This shows 'idlaal' cannot mean creating error.
Both Jabriyya and Qadariyya agree the servant cannot create error.
4
Reflect on the opposite relationship
Just as 'hidaayah' (guidance) does not force acceptance, 'idlaal' does not force error. One can say 'I guided him but he did not accept' and likewise 'I led him astray but he did not go astray'.
This logical symmetry proves 'idlaal' is not causative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: دلت الدلائل على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من هذه الآيات خلق الكفر والضلال، وإذا كان كذلك، وجب المصير إلى التأويل.

أما بيان المقام الأول: فمن وجوه:
الأول: إنا سنذكر الدلائل العقلية والنقلية على أنه لا يجوز من الله تعالى أن يخلق الكفر والجهل في المكلف.
الثاني: إن تفسير الإضلال بخلق الجهل غير جائز بحسب اللغة. أما أولاً: فلأن من منع غيره من سلوك طريق كرهاً وجبراً، فإنه لا يقال في اللغة الصحيحة: إنه أضله عن الطريق، بل يقال: إنه منعه وصرف عنه، وإنما يقال: أضله عن الطريق إذا لم يرد عليه وارد من الشبه، ما لأجله التبس عليه الصواب. وأما ثانيها: فلأنه تعالى وصف إبليس وفرعون بكون كل واحد منهما مضلاً. قال تعالى حكاية عن إبليس: {ولأضلنهم}، وقال تعالى: {وأضل فرعون قومه}. ثم إنا توافقنا على أنهما ما كانا خالقين للضلال في قلوب من أتبعهما. أما عند الجبرية فلأن العبد لا يقدر على الإيجاد، وأما عند القدرية فلأن العبد لا يقدر على هذا النوع من الإيجاد. ولما حصل اسم الضلال في هذه السورة، مع أنه لم يحصل فيها خلق الجهل والضلال، علمنا: أن الإضلال غير موضوع في اللغة لخلق الضلال. والمراد بالإضلال: التخلية ومنع الألطاف. وبالهداية: التوفيق واللطف. فكان ذلك كناية عن الكفر والإيمان. وأما ثالثها: فلأن الإضلال في مقابلة الهداية، فكما صح أن يقال: هديته فما اهتدى، وجب صحة أن يقال: أضللته فما ضل. وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع حمل لفظ الإضلال على خلق الإضلال.

الوجه الثالث في بيان أن لفظ الإضلال في هذه الآيات لا يمكن حمله على خلق الضلال: هو أن هذا التفسير لا يليق بهذه الآيات الخمس. وذلك لأن قوله تعالى: {وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم فيضل الله من يشاء} - وكيف يجوز أن يقال: إني لم أرسل رسولاً إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم الغرض من التكاليف، ثم يقول بعده: إني أضللتهم عن الدين؟ فإنه لا تعلق لهذا الكلام بما قبله، ويصير الكلام ركيكاً.

فثبت بهذه الوجوه: أنه لا يمكن أن يكون المراد من قوله {يضل من يشاء} خلق الكفر والجهل فيهم. وإذا ظهر هذا، علمنا أن المراد منه شيء آخر. وحينئذ لا نحتاج إلى تعيين ذلك المراد في مقام الجدل.
If it is said: The evidences indicate that it is not permissible that what is meant by these verses is the creation of disbelief and error, and if this is so, then resorting to interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary.

As for the explanation of the first station: it is [based on] several points:
First: We will mention rational and transmitted proofs that it is not permissible for Allah Almighty to create disbelief and ignorance in the accountable person.
Second: The interpretation of 'idlaal' as creating ignorance is not permissible according to language. As for first: because if someone prevents another from following a path by force and compulsion, it is not said in correct Arabic that he led him astray from the path; rather, it is said that he prevented and turned him away. Rather, one says 'he led him astray from the path' only when no doubt (shubha) occurs to him due to which the correct becomes confused for him. As for second: because Allah Almighty described Iblis and Pharaoh as each being one who leads astray (mudill). Allah Almighty said, quoting Iblis: {And I will surely lead them astray}, and said: {And Pharaoh led his people astray}. Then we all agree that they were not creators of error in the hearts of their followers. According to the Jabriyya, the servant is not able to create; according to the Qadariyya, the servant is not able to create this type of creation. Since the word 'error' appears in this sura, while it does not contain the creation of ignorance and error, we know that 'idlaal' is not lexically assigned to mean creation of error. And what is meant by 'idlaal' is abandonment (takhliya) and withholding of divine graces (man' al-altaaf). And by 'hidaayah' is success-giving (tawfeeq) and grace (lutf). So that is a metaphor for disbelief and faith. As for third: because 'idlaal' is opposite of 'hidaayah'. Just as it is correct to say 'I guided him, and he did not accept guidance', so too it must be correct to say 'I led him astray, and he did not go astray'. If this is so, it is impossible to apply the word 'idlaal' to the creation of error.

Third point: To show that the word 'idlaal' in these verses cannot be interpreted as creating error: it is that this interpretation does not suit these five verses. That is because Allah's saying: {And We did not send any messenger except in the language of his people to make clear to them, so Allah leads astray whom He wills} – how can it be said: 'I did not send a messenger except in the language of his people to make clear to them the purpose of obligations, then He says after that: I led them astray from the religion'? For this speech has no connection to what precedes it, and the speech becomes weak.

Thus it is established by these points that it is impossible that what is meant by His saying {He leads astray whom He wills} is the creation of disbelief and ignorance in them. When this is clear, we know that what is meant is something else. In that case, we do not need to specify that meaning in a polemical context (al-jadal).
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author first presents an objection: since rational proofs show that Allah cannot create disbelief and error, the verses containing 'yudillu man yashaa' cannot be taken literally, so ta'wil (allegorical interpretation) is necessary. The author then agrees but provides three proofs that the word 'idlaal' cannot literally mean 'creating error'.

**First proof**: The author promises to present rational and transmitted proofs against Allah's creating error, but does not elaborate here.

**Second proof**: Linguistic argument with three subpoints:
   a) If someone is forced away from a path, one does not say 'he led him astray' in Arabic, but rather 'he prevented him'. 'Idlaal' is only used when confusion induced by doubts occurs.
   b) Allah calls Iblis and Pharaoh 'mudillun' (leaders astray), yet both were not creators of error in the hearts of followers. The Jabriyya hold the servant incapable of creation; the Qadariyya hold the servant incapable of this type of creation. Since the term 'error' appears in the sura without any creation of error, 'idlaal' cannot mean creating error. The actual meaning is 'abandonment and withholding of graces' and 'hidaayah' is 'granting success and grace'.
   c) 'Idlaal' is opposite of 'hidaayah'. Just as one can say 'I guided him but he did not accept guidance', so too one can say 'I led him astray but he did not go astray'. Hence, 'idlaal' cannot mean causing error invariably.

**Third proof**: Contextual argument from the verse in Surah Ibrahim: Allah sends messengers to clarify, then says 'fa-yudillu Allahu man yashaa'. It would be incoherent to say that after sending a messenger to clarify, Allah then creates error in them. That would make the speech disjointed.

**Conclusion**: Based on these proofs, the phrase 'yudillu man yashaa' cannot mean creating disbelief and ignorance. The actual meaning is something else, but in a polemical context one need not specify that meaning.

Key Terms
إضلال
ضلال
هداية
خلق
جبرية
قدرية
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the word 'idlaal' in the Quran literally means Allah creates error in the hearts of people.
Correction: In correct Arabic, 'idlaal' is used only when a doubt confuses the path, not for direct creation. The author shows through linguistic usage, Quranic examples, and logical symmetry that 'idlaal' cannot mean creating error.

Checkpoint
According to the author's second proof's first subpoint, when is it linguistically correct to say someone 'led another astray'?
A) When the person is forced away from the path by compulsion.
B) When a doubt (shubha) occurs that confuses the correct path for the person.
C) When the person is prevented from the path with clear evidence.
D) When the person's own choice is completely absent.

Key Takeaway
The word 'idlaal' in Arabic cannot mean creating error; it refers to abandoning someone to confusion, as shown by linguistic rules, Quranic analogies, and the opposite relationship with guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'yudillu man yashaa', what assumption did you bring to it? How does this linguistic clarification change your attitude towards Allah's justice? Will you now pause before interpreting ambiguous terms in the Qur'an based on preconceived notions?
?
The author argues that 'idlaal' means abandoning and withholding grace. Do you truly believe that when people go astray, it is because they were left without divine grace? How does this affect your view of those who commit sin—do you see them as victims of withheld grace, or do you still hold them accountable? What does this mean for your own self-righteousness?
?
Just as guidance is not causative (one can say 'hedaitehu falam yahtadi'), misguidance is also not causative. When you see someone persisting in error despite your advice, do you blame yourself or God? Or do you accept that even your best efforts are not causative? How does this lesson free you from false guilt or false pride?

Contextual Proof that 'Yudill' Does Not Mean Creating Error
Contextual proof from Quranic verse that 'idlaal' cannot mean creating error, and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contextual proof from the Quranic verse in Surah Ibrahim that shows 'yudillu man yashaa' cannot mean creating error, and why the author concludes that the actual meaning is something else.

Core Idea
A verse that speaks of sending messengers to clarify cannot then mean that Allah creates error in those same people—it would be incoherent.

The author provides a third proof focusing on the verse itself (14:4). He argues that interpreting 'yudillu' as creating error would make the verse disjointed, because the first part says Allah sent messengers to explain, and the second part would then say He creates error. This lesson examines that argument and the conclusion that the real meaning is something else, though not specified in polemical context.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the passage where the author argues that interpreting 'idlaal' as creating error does not suit the five verses, particularly the verse from Surah Ibrahim.
2
Understand the contextual contradiction
The verse says Allah sent messengers to clarify, then says 'fa-yudillu Allahu man yashaa'. If 'yudillu' meant creating error, the message would be incoherent: after clarification, He creates error? That would make the speech weak and disjointed.
The author emphasizes that such an interpretation breaks the connection between the two parts of the verse.
3
See the conclusion
The author concludes that based on all proofs, 'yudillu man yashaa' cannot mean creating disbelief and ignorance. The actual meaning is something else, but in a polemical context, specifying that meaning is not necessary.
The focus is on refuting the false interpretation, not presenting the correct one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: دلت الدلائل على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من هذه الآيات خلق الكفر والضلال، وإذا كان كذلك، وجب المصير إلى التأويل.

أما بيان المقام الأول: فمن وجوه:
الأول: إنا سنذكر الدلائل العقلية والنقلية على أنه لا يجوز من الله تعالى أن يخلق الكفر والجهل في المكلف.
الثاني: إن تفسير الإضلال بخلق الجهل غير جائز بحسب اللغة. أما أولاً: فلأن من منع غيره من سلوك طريق كرهاً وجبراً، فإنه لا يقال في اللغة الصحيحة: إنه أضله عن الطريق، بل يقال: إنه منعه وصرف عنه، وإنما يقال: أضله عن الطريق إذا لم يرد عليه وارد من الشبه، ما لأجله التبس عليه الصواب. وأما ثانيها: فلأنه تعالى وصف إبليس وفرعون بكون كل واحد منهما مضلاً. قال تعالى حكاية عن إبليس: {ولأضلنهم}، وقال تعالى: {وأضل فرعون قومه}. ثم إنا توافقنا على أنهما ما كانا خالقين للضلال في قلوب من أتبعهما. أما عند الجبرية فلأن العبد لا يقدر على الإيجاد، وأما عند القدرية فلأن العبد لا يقدر على هذا النوع من الإيجاد. ولما حصل اسم الضلال في هذه السورة، مع أنه لم يحصل فيها خلق الجهل والضلال، علمنا: أن الإضلال غير موضوع في اللغة لخلق الضلال. والمراد بالإضلال: التخلية ومنع الألطاف. وبالهداية: التوفيق واللطف. فكان ذلك كناية عن الكفر والإيمان. وأما ثالثها: فلأن الإضلال في مقابلة الهداية، فكما صح أن يقال: هديته فما اهتدى، وجب صحة أن يقال: أضللته فما ضل. وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع حمل لفظ الإضلال على خلق الإضلال.

الوجه الثالث في بيان أن لفظ الإضلال في هذه الآيات لا يمكن حمله على خلق الضلال: هو أن هذا التفسير لا يليق بهذه الآيات الخمس. وذلك لأن قوله تعالى: {وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم فيضل الله من يشاء} - وكيف يجوز أن يقال: إني لم أرسل رسولاً إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم الغرض من التكاليف، ثم يقول بعده: إني أضللتهم عن الدين؟ فإنه لا تعلق لهذا الكلام بما قبله، ويصير الكلام ركيكاً.

فثبت بهذه الوجوه: أنه لا يمكن أن يكون المراد من قوله {يضل من يشاء} خلق الكفر والجهل فيهم. وإذا ظهر هذا، علمنا أن المراد منه شيء آخر. وحينئذ لا نحتاج إلى تعيين ذلك المراد في مقام الجدل.
If it is said: The evidences indicate that it is not permissible that what is meant by these verses is the creation of disbelief and error, and if this is so, then resorting to interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary.

As for the explanation of the first station: it is [based on] several points:
First: We will mention rational and transmitted proofs that it is not permissible for Allah Almighty to create disbelief and ignorance in the accountable person.
Second: The interpretation of 'idlaal' as creating ignorance is not permissible according to language. As for first: because if someone prevents another from following a path by force and compulsion, it is not said in correct Arabic that he led him astray from the path; rather, it is said that he prevented and turned him away. Rather, one says 'he led him astray from the path' only when no doubt (shubha) occurs to him due to which the correct becomes confused for him. As for second: because Allah Almighty described Iblis and Pharaoh as each being one who leads astray (mudill). Allah Almighty said, quoting Iblis: {And I will surely lead them astray}, and said: {And Pharaoh led his people astray}. Then we all agree that they were not creators of error in the hearts of their followers. According to the Jabriyya, the servant is not able to create; according to the Qadariyya, the servant is not able to create this type of creation. Since the word 'error' appears in this sura, while it does not contain the creation of ignorance and error, we know that 'idlaal' is not lexically assigned to mean creation of error. And what is meant by 'idlaal' is abandonment (takhliya) and withholding of divine graces (man' al-altaaf). And by 'hidaayah' is success-giving (tawfeeq) and grace (lutf). So that is a metaphor for disbelief and faith. As for third: because 'idlaal' is opposite of 'hidaayah'. Just as it is correct to say 'I guided him, and he did not accept guidance', so too it must be correct to say 'I led him astray, and he did not go astray'. If this is so, it is impossible to apply the word 'idlaal' to the creation of error.

Third point: To show that the word 'idlaal' in these verses cannot be interpreted as creating error: it is that this interpretation does not suit these five verses. That is because Allah's saying: {And We did not send any messenger except in the language of his people to make clear to them, so Allah leads astray whom He wills} – how can it be said: 'I did not send a messenger except in the language of his people to make clear to them the purpose of obligations, then He says after that: I led them astray from the religion'? For this speech has no connection to what precedes it, and the speech becomes weak.

Thus it is established by these points that it is impossible that what is meant by His saying {He leads astray whom He wills} is the creation of disbelief and ignorance in them. When this is clear, we know that what is meant is something else. In that case, we do not need to specify that meaning in a polemical context (al-jadal).
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The author first presents an objection: since rational proofs show that Allah cannot create disbelief and error, the verses containing 'yudillu man yashaa' cannot be taken literally, so ta'wil (allegorical interpretation) is necessary. The author then agrees but provides three proofs that the word 'idlaal' cannot literally mean 'creating error'.

**First proof**: The author promises to present rational and transmitted proofs against Allah's creating error, but does not elaborate here.

**Second proof**: Linguistic argument with three subpoints:
   a) If someone is forced away from a path, one does not say 'he led him astray' in Arabic, but rather 'he prevented him'. 'Idlaal' is only used when confusion induced by doubts occurs.
   b) Allah calls Iblis and Pharaoh 'mudillun' (leaders astray), yet both were not creators of error in the hearts of followers. The Jabriyya hold the servant incapable of creation; the Qadariyya hold the servant incapable of this type of creation. Since the term 'error' appears in the sura without any creation of error, 'idlaal' cannot mean creating error. The actual meaning is 'abandonment and withholding of graces' and 'hidaayah' is 'granting success and grace'.
   c) 'Idlaal' is opposite of 'hidaayah'. Just as one can say 'I guided him but he did not accept guidance', so too one can say 'I led him astray but he did not go astray'. Hence, 'idlaal' cannot mean causing error invariably.

**Third proof**: Contextual argument from the verse in Surah Ibrahim: Allah sends messengers to clarify, then says 'fa-yudillu Allahu man yashaa'. It would be incoherent to say that after sending a messenger to clarify, Allah then creates error in them. That would make the speech disjointed.

**Conclusion**: Based on these proofs, the phrase 'yudillu man yashaa' cannot mean creating disbelief and ignorance. The actual meaning is something else, but in a polemical context one need not specify that meaning.

Key Terms
إضلال
ضلال
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'وما أرسلنا من رسول... فيضل الله من يشاء' can be interpreted literally as Allah creating error in people after sending a messenger.
Correction: Such an interpretation makes the verse incoherent because it would mean Allah sends a messenger to clarify, then creates error in them. The author shows this is disconnected and weak, so the word must have a different meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the interpretation of 'yudillu' as creating error does not suit the verse from Surah Ibrahim?
A) Because the verse mentions messengers sent to their own people.
B) Because the verse would then say Allah sends messengers to clarify, then creates error, making the speech disjointed.
C) Because the verse is about the Arabic language.
D) Because the verse is abrogated by other verses.

Key Takeaway
The contextual proof from Surah Ibrahim shows that interpreting 'yudillu' as creating error makes the verse incoherent, so the true meaning must be something else, which the author does not need to specify in a polemical argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic verse that seems to challenge your understanding, do you rush to impose a meaning that fits your preconceptions, even if it creates internal contradictions? How willing are you to let the text question your assumptions and humbly admit you might need to learn from the ulama?
?
The author shows that once a false interpretation is refuted, there is no need to specify the correct meaning in polemics. Where in your own life do you feel the pressure to always have a full explanation, rather than simply trusting Allah’s wisdom and letting go of the need to prove yourself right?
?
The verse reminds us that messengers were sent to clarify. Have you ever used your speech, even in casual conversation, to ‘clarify’ something about Islam when you were not qualified, potentially leading others astray? What would it look like for you to commit to only speaking about Allah with knowledge and to remain silent when you don’t know?

First Interpretation of 'Yudill': Occasion of Misguidance
The author presents the first ta'wil of 'yudill' as occasion-based misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first ta'wil of 'yudill' as an occasion-based figurative expression, and how it applies to Allah's attribute of leading astray.

Core Idea
Allah's 'misguidance' is the occasion for people's self-chosen error.

This lesson explores the author's first interpretive proposal for the verses mentioning Allah's leading astray. He argues that when someone goes astray in the presence of something, that thing is figuratively called a 'misleader'. The same logic applies to Allah: He is the occasion for misguidance when people choose error in circumstances He provides. We will examine how this interpretation uses Quranic verses about idols to support its plausibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the author's main point.
Notice the pattern: 'when a person goes astray in the presence of X, X is said to have led him astray'.
2
Understand the core concept: occasion vs. cause
The author distinguishes between something being the direct cause of error and being merely the occasion (the context) in which a person freely chooses error. The idols did not create disbelief; they were present when people chose disbelief.
This is a key distinction in kalam discussions about divine attributes.
3
Examine the Quranic examples
The author cites three verses: Ibrahim's statement about idols, the mention of Yaghuth, Ya'uq, and Nasr, and a partial verse about revelation. For each, he explains that 'they led astray' means 'people went astray at the sight of them'.
The third verse is incomplete in the source but continues the same idea.
4
Reflect on application to Allah
Ask yourself: How does this ta'wil help reconcile the verse 'Allah leads astray whom He wills' with human responsibility? The answer is that Allah's 'leading astray' is the occasion, not the creation, of error.
This avoids attributing evil directly to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نبين وجوهاً كثيرة تحتملها هذه الآية:
فالتأويل الأول: إن الرجل إذا ضل باختياره عند حضور شيء من غير أن يكون لذلك أثر في ضلاله، فيقال لذلك الشيء: إنه أضله. قال تعالى في حق الأصنام: {رب إنهن أضللن كثيراً من الناس} أي ضلوا عند رؤيتها. وقال تعالى: {ولا يغوث ويعوق ونسرا وقد أضلوا كثيراً} أي ضل كثير من الناس عند رؤيتهم. وقال: {وليزيدن كثيراً منهم ما أنزل إليك}
Then we will explain many possibilities that this verse bears:
The first interpretation (ta'wil): A man, when he goes astray by his own choice in the presence of something, while that thing has no effect in his going astray, then that thing is said to have led him astray (adalla). Allah Almighty said concerning idols: {Lord, indeed they have led many people astray}, meaning they went astray at the sight of them. And He said: {Nor Yaghuth, nor Ya'uq, nor Nasr, and they have led many astray}, meaning many people went astray at the sight of them. And He said: {And what was sent down to you will increase many of them}
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is an interpretive proposal (ta'wil). After proving that 'idlaal' cannot mean literal creation of error, the author offers possible alternative meanings. Here he presents the first one: when a person freely chooses to go astray in the presence of some object (like an idol), that object is figuratively called the 'misleader' (mudill), even though it had no causal effect. The author cites Quranic examples: Ibrahim's speech about idols ('they have led many astray') and the mention of pre-Islamic idols (Yaghuth, Ya'uq, Nasr) in Surah Nuh, where the idols are said to have led many astray. In both cases, the meaning is that people went astray when they saw or worshipped those idols, not that the idols themselves created error. The third example is cut off but continues a similar point: revelation may 'increase' many in error, meaning it becomes the occasion for their existing misguidance.

Thus, the phrase 'yudillu man yashaa' (Allah leads astray whom He wills) can be understood in this metaphorical sense: Allah is the ultimate occasion for misguidance when He wills, by allowing circumstances in which people choose error, but not by creating that error directly.

Key Terms
إضلال
تأويل
أصنام
ضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this ta'wil makes Allah the direct cause of error.
Correction: It only makes Him an occasion for what people choose; the error is self-chosen.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'أضللن كثيراً من الناس' (they led many people astray) according to the author's first interpretation?
A) The idols created disbelief in the people.
B) The idols prevented the people from the truth by force.
C) The people went astray at the sight of the idols, by their own choice.
D) The idols confused the people with doubts.

Key Takeaway
The first ta'wil interprets 'yudill' as describing the occasion for self-chosen misguidance, not the creation of error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed something external—a person, a place, or a circumstance—for your own spiritual decline? How does this ta'wil challenge that excuse and call you to own your choices?
?
When you find yourself slipping in your practice of Islam, do you instinctively look for an external cause rather than examining your own free will? What would it cost you today to take full responsibility for your state?
?
Consider something you often say 'leads me astray'—a habit, a device, even a relationship. If nothing external has any causal effect on your misguidance, what does that demand from you personally? Will you still treat that thing as an enemy, or now face yourself?

The Incoherence of Interpreting 'Idlaal' as Creating Misguidance
Author argues that interpreting 'idlaal' as creating misguidance makes Qur'anic verses incoherent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting 'idlaal' as creating misguidance leads to logical inconsistency in the Qur'anic verses.

Core Idea
If God sends messengers to clarify, then misguides them, the speech becomes self-contradictory.

In this lesson, we examine the third argument from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah against the Ash'ari interpretation of 'idlaal' as creating misguidance. The author points to a specific verse and shows that taking 'idlaal' literally would make the divine speech disjointed and illogical. This argument relies on the coherence of the Qur'anic text itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise argumentative style.
2
Identify the key contradiction
The author cites Quran 14:4: God sends messengers to clarify, then says 'Allah misguides whom He wills.' Ask yourself: why is this problematic if 'idlaal' means creating misguidance?
Focus on the sequence: clarification followed by misguidance.
3
Understand why the speech becomes flimsy
The author argues that if God's purpose in sending a messenger is to clarify obligations, then saying He misled them immediately after makes the speech disconnected (рукка). Reflect on why this is a logical problem.
Coherence of divine speech is a key principle in kalam.
4
Connect to the broader theological debate
This argument is part of a larger critique against the Ash'ari view that God creates all actions, including misguidance. Consider how this passage supports the opposing view that 'idlaal' means something else (e.g., judgment or abandonment).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإضلال الوجه الثالث في بيان أن لفظ الإضلال في هذه الآيات لا يمكن حمله على خلق الضلال . هو أن هذا التفسير لا يليق بهذه الآيات الخمس . وذلك لأن قوله تعالى ( وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم فيضل الله من يشاء ) وكيف يجوز أن يقال : إني لم أرسل رسولاً إلا بلسان قومه ليبين لهم الغرض من التكاليف ، ثم يقول بعده : إني أضللتهم عن الدين ؟ فإنه لا تعلق لهذا الكلام بما قبله ، ويصير الكلام ركيكاً
The third aspect in explaining that the word 'idlaal' in these verses cannot be interpreted as creating misguidance. It is that this interpretation does not suit these five verses. That is because Allah's saying: 'And We did not send any messenger except in the language of his people to make clear to them; then Allah misguides whom He wills' — and how can it be said: 'I did not send a messenger except in the language of his people to make clear to them the purpose of obligations, then after it I say: I misled them away from the religion?' For there is no connection of this speech to what precedes it, and the speech becomes flimsy.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against interpreting 'idlaal' as creating misguidance. The author states that this interpretation does not fit the five verses in question. He specifically cites the verse from Surah Ibrahim (14:4): 'And We did not send any messenger except in the language of his people to make clear to them; then Allah misguides whom He wills.' He points out the contradiction: if God sends a messenger to clarify obligations, then immediately afterwards says He misled them, the speech becomes disconnected and logically unsound. Thus, the interpretation is invalid.

Key Terms
إضلال
خلق الضلال
الآيات الخمس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'idlaal' must mean creating misguidance.
Correction: The author shows that this interpretation would make the verse from Surah Ibrahim incoherent, because the first part states God sent a messenger to clarify, and the second part would then say He misled them, which is logically contradictory.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with interpreting 'idlaal' as creating misguidance in the verse from Surah Ibrahim?
A) It contradicts the idea that God is merciful.
B) It makes the speech disconnected because God first sends a messenger to clarify, then says He misled.
C) It is impossible for God to create misguidance.
D) The verse does not use the word 'idlaal'.

Key Takeaway
Interpreting 'idlaal' as creating misguidance contradicts the purpose of sending messengers, making divine speech illogical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear verses about Allah misguiding whom He wills, do you ever feel that your own efforts to seek guidance are futile, as if your misguidance could be created regardless? How does this lesson's argument about the logical coherence of Allah's speech challenge that feeling? What concrete step will you take this week to trust that Allah's sending of the message is a genuine call for you to respond, not a pretense?
?
Have you ever used the concept of qadar as an excuse to avoid striving in obedience, thinking 'if Allah will guide me He will, regardless'? This lesson shows that such thinking makes Allah's speech illogical. What area of your life have you been passive about due to this misconception? Write down one action you will commit to today to actively seek guidance through the means Allah provided.
?
The author argues that interpreting 'idlaal' as creating misguidance makes Allah's speech 'flimsy.' How does your understanding of Allah's wisdom and justice require that His actions be internally consistent? Recall a time when you doubted Allah's fairness because of a misunderstood verse. How does this lesson's method of examining logical coherence help you resolve such doubts?

Conclusion: The Method of Dialectic
Author concludes that the intended meaning is not creating disbelief and dialectic does not require specifying it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author concludes his arguments on the verse 'He misguides whom He wills' and why specifying the correct interpretation is unnecessary in dialectic.

Core Idea
In dialectic, refuting a false interpretation is sufficient without needing to specify the true one.

In this final passage, the author draws together his earlier arguments to establish that the intended meaning of divine misguidance cannot be the creation of disbelief. Once this negative conclusion is reached, he makes a key methodological point: in the context of dialectic (jadal), we are not required to identify the correct positive meaning. This clarifies the scope of theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Recap the preceding arguments
The passage opens with 'Thus it is established by these ways', referring back to the arguments given earlier. Reflect on how the author built his case.
Consider the previous lesson's proofs.
2
Identify the negative conclusion
The author states that the intended meaning cannot be the creation of disbelief and ignorance. Understand this as a refutation of a specific interpretation.
3
Grasp the methodological point
The author says we know the meaning is something else but don't need to specify it in dialectic. This is a crucial point about the limits of debate.
This shows the sufficiency of negative argumentation.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider why it might be tempting to demand a positive alternative. The author's approach shows that refuting a false interpretation can be sufficient in argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت بهذه الوجوه : أنه لا يمكن أن يكون المراد من قوله ( يضل من يشاء ) خلق الكفر والجهل فيهم. وإذا ظهر هذا ، علمنا أن المراد منه شيء آخر . وحينئذ لا نحتاج إلى تعيين ذلك المراد في مقام الجدل
Thus it is established by these ways that it cannot be that the intended meaning of His saying 'He misguides whom He wills' is the creation of disbelief and ignorance in them. And when this is clear, we know that the intended meaning is something else. And then we do not need to specify that intended meaning in the context of dialectic.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This short passage is the conclusion of the preceding arguments. The author states that it is established by these ways (the arguments he has given) that the intended meaning of God's saying 'He misguides whom He wills' cannot be the creation of disbelief and ignorance in them. Once this is clear, we know that the intended meaning is something else. And at this point, in the context of dialectic (jadal), we do not need to specify exactly what that meaning is; it is sufficient to have refuted the false interpretation.

Key Terms
يضل من يشاء
خلق الكفر والجهل
مقام الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: In theological debate, after refuting a false interpretation, one must immediately provide the correct interpretation.
Correction: The author clarifies that in dialectic, it is sufficient to refute the false interpretation; specifying the correct meaning is not required at that stage.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is required in the context of dialectic after establishing that the intended meaning is not the creation of disbelief?
A) To specify the correct meaning from consensus
B) To not specify the intended meaning
C) To affirm the literal meaning
D) To seek further arguments

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, refuting a false interpretation is sufficient; one need not immediately supply the alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue with someone about a matter of faith, do you feel pressured to have all the answers? How does the author's principle—that refuting falsehood is enough in dialectic—free you from that burden? Will you trust that sometimes refuting a wrong belief is sufficient without immediately offering the correct one?
?
This lesson refutes the idea that Allah creates disbelief in people. Have you ever, even silently, blamed Allah for someone's disbelief to avoid personal responsibility or to make sense of the world? How does this lesson correct that? How will you respond differently when you encounter someone who is misguided?

Applying Temporal Association to Guidance and Misguidance
Author applies the temporal association principle to explain God's attribution of guidance and misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of temporal association is used to explain why guidance and misguidance are attributed to Allah, even though the servant creates them.

Core Idea
Guidance is attributed to Allah by temporal coincidence, not direct creation.

We have learned the principle that things can be attributed to Allah based on temporal association. Now the author applies this to the critical issue of divine guidance and misguidance. This passage shows how this principle preserves both divine attribution and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the structure: 'If you have understood this, then we say...'
2
Identify the principle at work
The attribution is based on temporal association: guidance occurs at the time of a specific action by Allah. Distinguish this from direct causation.
The key phrase is 'at the time of a specific action performed by Allah'.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
This explanation preserves both God's ultimate role and human responsibility. The servant is the real agent, but attribution to Allah is valid due to temporal coincidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنما أضيف الهدى والإضلال إلى الله تعالى على هذا الوجه ، وذلك لأنهما لو كانا يحدثان من العبد عند فعل مخصوص يفعله الله تعالى ، لا جرم أضيفا إلى الله تعالى ، وإن كانا في الحقيقة إنما يحصلان بإيجاد العبد وتكوينه
If you have understood this, then we say: guidance and misguidance are only attributed to Allah Almighty in this manner, and that is because if they occur from the servant at the time of a specific action performed by Allah Almighty, then they are necessarily attributed to Allah Almighty, even though in reality they come about only through the servant's creative act and formation.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage applies the previously established principle to the divine attribution of guidance and misguidance. The author says: if you have understood this (the first interpretation where attribution is based on temporal association, not causation), then we say that guidance (huda) and misguidance (idlaal) are attributed to Allah in this same manner. This is because if they occur from the servant at the time of a specific action performed by Allah, then they are necessarily attributed to Allah, even though in reality they come about through the servant's own creative act (ijaad) and formation (takween). This preserves God's role as the ultimate cause while maintaining human responsibility.

Key Terms

إيجاد العبد
تكوينه

Misconception: Some might think that attributing guidance to Allah implies He creates it directly in the servant.
Correction: The author clarifies that the attribution is based on temporal association, not direct causation. The servant is the real agent through his creative act.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is guidance attributed to Allah even though the servant creates it?
A) Because Allah creates it directly.
B) Because it occurs at the time of an action performed by Allah.
C) Because the servant cannot create anything.
D) Because guidance is a divine attribute.

Key Takeaway
Guidance and misguidance are attributed to Allah due to temporal association, not direct causation.

Second Interpretation: 'Idlaal' as Naming Misguided
Author presents second interpretation: 'idlaal' means to name/judge as misguided

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'idlaal' can mean naming or judging someone as misguided, rather than causing misguidance.

Core Idea
'Idlaal' can mean to call someone misguided, not make them misguided.

In debates about divine decree, 'idlaal' often implies causing misguidance. However, the author presents a second interpretation where 'idlaal' means to name or judge someone as misguided. This shifts the focus from divine causation to human judgment, supported by linguistic analogy and poetry.

Guided Path
1

The term 'tasmia' is crucial – it means calling or naming.
2

The author compares 'he misguided him' to 'he called him a disbeliever'. This shows that 'idlaal' can be a verbal judgment, not an act of creation.
The word 'hukm' (judgment) is used in the same way.
3
Analyze the poetic evidence
The line by al-Kumayt illustrates how 'declaring someone a disbeliever' is similar to 'naming someone misguided'. Consider how this supports the interpretation.
Poetry was often used in classical arguments to show common linguistic usage.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Think about how this interpretation affects the qadar debate: if 'idlaal' only means judging, then God's 'idlaal' is not creating misguidance but labeling those who choose misguidance.

First Interpretation: Misguidance Attributed to Occasion
Author presents first interpretation: misguidance attributed to occasion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretation of divine misguidance in the Qur'an, where attribution is based on temporal coincidence rather than causation.

Core Idea
When the Qur'an says something 'misled' people, it can mean they went astray at that time, not because of it.

The author of al-Matalib al-Aliyah begins his discussion of the different interpretations of 'idlaal' by presenting a key principle: attribution in the Qur'an often follows temporal association, not causal efficacy. This interpretation resolves apparent conflicts between divine sovereignty and human responsibility. Let us explore this principle through several Qur'anic examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author states the principle first, then supports it with multiple examples.
2
Identify the pattern
In each example, ask: what is the relationship between the attributed agent (idols, revelation, etc.) and the outcome? The author insists they merely coincide.
3
Examine the special case
The example of mockers making people forget is explained: they did not cause forgetfulness, but their mockery was a cause. How does this refine the principle?
4
Apply the principle
Consider the surah al-Tawba example. How does revelation increase both faith and impurity? The author says it is because states change at the time of revelation.
5
Summarise
Write a one-sentence summary of the first interpretation in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالتأويل الأول : إن الرجل إذا ضل باختياره عند حضور شيء من غير أن يكون لذلك أثر في ضلاله . فيقال لذلك الشيء : إنه أضله . قال تعالى في حق الأصنام : رب أنهن أضللن كثيراً من الناس ( أي ضلوا عند رؤيتها . وقال تعالى : ( ولا يغوث ويعوق ونسرا . وقد أضلوا كثيراً ) أي ضل كثير من الناس عند رؤيتهم. وقال : ( وليزيدن كثيراً منهم ما أنزل إليك من ربك طغياناً وكفر ) وقال أيضاً : فلم يزدهم دعائي إلا قراراً ) أي لم يزدادوا بدعائي لهم إلا قراراً . وقال أيضاً : ( فاتخذتموهم سخرياً ، حق أنسوكم ذكرى ) ومعلوم : أنهم لم ينسوهم في الحقيقة ، بل كانوا يذكرونهم الله ويدعونهم إليه. لكن لما كان استغناؤهم بالسخرية منهم سبباً في نسيانهم ، أضيف النسيان إليهم. وقال أيضاً في سورة التوبة : ( وإذا ما أنزلت سورة ، فمنهم من يقول : أيكم زادته هذه إيمانا . فأما الذين آمنوا فزادتهم إيماناً ، وهم يستبشرون . وأما الذين في قلوبهم مرض ، فزادتهم رجسا إلى رجسهم )) فأخبر سبحانه : أن نزول السورة المشتملة على الشرائع تفرق أحوالهم . فمنهم من يصلح عند نزولها ، فيزدادوا إيماناً ، ومنهم من يفسد حينئذ ، فيزدادوا كفراً . فلا جرم أضيفت الزيادة في الإيمان، والزيادة في الكفر ، إلى السورة لأجل أن تلك الزيادة إنما حصلت عند نزول تلك السورة
The first interpretation: A man, if he goes astray by his own choice at the presence of something, without that thing having any effect in his misguidance, then that thing is said to have misled him. Allah said regarding idols: 'My Lord, indeed they have misled many people' (i.e., they went astray when they saw them). And He said: 'And neither Yaghuth nor Ya'uq nor Nasr. And they have misled many' (i.e., many people went astray when they saw them). And He said: 'And that what has been revealed to you from your Lord will increase many of them in transgression and disbelief.' And He also said: 'And my calling did not increase them except in flight' (i.e., my calling them did not increase them except in flight). And He also said: 'And you took them as mockery, until they made you forget My remembrance.' And it is known: they did not actually make them forget, but rather they would remind them of Allah and call them to Him. However, because their self-sufficiency through mockery of them was a cause for their forgetfulness, forgetfulness was attributed to them. And He said in Surat al-Tawba: 'And whenever a surah is revealed, among them are those who say: Which of you has this increased in faith? As for those who believed, it increased them in faith, and they rejoice. And as for those whose hearts are diseased, it increased them in impurity upon their impurity.' So the Exalted informed that the revelation of a surah containing the laws differentiates their states. Among them are those who mend at its revelation, so they increase in faith, and among them are those who spoil at that time, so they increase in disbelief. Necessarily, the increase in faith and the increase in disbelief were attributed to the surah because that increase only occurred at the revelation of that surah.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents the first possible interpretation of the word 'idlaal' in the Qur'an. The author explains that when a person goes astray by his own choice at the time of encountering something, that thing is said to have misled him, even if it had no real effect on his misguidance. He provides several Qur'anic examples: the idols being said to have misled many people (Surah Ibrahim 14:36); the false gods of Noah (Surah Nuh 71:23-24) meaning people went astray when they saw them; the revelation increasing transgression (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:68?); Noah's calling increasing flight (Surah Nuh 71:6); the mockers making people forget God's remembrance (Surah al-Mu'minun 23:110) — here the author clarifies that they did not actually cause forgetfulness, but their mockery was a cause, so forgetfulness was attributed to them; and the example from Surah al-Tawba (9:124-125) where a revealed surah increases faith for believers but increases impurity for those with diseased hearts. In all these cases, the attribution is based on temporal coincidence, not causation. The increase in faith or disbelief is attributed to the surah because it occurred at the time of its revelation.

Key Terms
التأويل الأول
ضل باختياره
عند حضور شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That when the Qur'an says something 'misled' people, it implies that thing caused the misguidance.
Correction: The first interpretation shows that such attribution can be based on temporal coincidence; the thing did not cause the misguidance, but the misguidance occurred at that time.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, why are the idols said to have misled many people?
A) Because the idols actively caused misguidance.
B) Because people went astray when they saw the idols.
C) Because the idols ordered people to go astray.
D) Because the idols created disbelief in their hearts.

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation of misguidance in the Qur'an is that attribution to an external cause can be purely based on temporal association, not causation, allowing for human responsibility.

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Rational Proof Against God Creating Disbelief
The author refutes the Mu'tazilite rational argument against God creating disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that rational proof prohibits God from creating disbelief, and his own rational proof that God is the preponderator of misguidance.

Core Idea
Rational proofs based on human moral intuition cannot limit God's actions.

This lesson addresses a crucial debate in Islamic theology: can rational arguments restrict God's power? The Mu'tazilites claimed that reason proves God cannot create disbelief in a servant. The author responds by exposing the weakness of their foundation—rational good and evil—and then presents a decisive proof that only God can cause the heart to incline.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before analyzing the structure.
Notice that the passage has two clear sections: refuting the opponent and offering the author's proof.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite objection
Identify what the Mu'tazilites claim and on what basis they build their argument. The author states the claim and then points to its foundation.
Key phrase: 'وذلك مبني على الحسن والقبح العقليين'.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author dismisses the Mu'tazilite rational proofs as being based on rational good and evil, which he says is extremely weak. Understand why he considers it weak in the context of divine action.
4
Examine the author's own proof
The author provides a rational proof: the inclination of the heart requires a preponderator, and that is God. See how he concludes this is a decisive proof.
Notice the logical necessity: no other explanation for the heart's turn to misguidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما قوله : الدلائل العقلية دلت على أنه لا يجوز أن يخلق الله الكفر في العبد ، قلنا : الدلائل العقلية التي يذكرونها لا تزيد على فعل المدح والذم . وذلك مبني على الحسن والقبح العقليين. وسنبين أن هذه القاعدة في غاية الضعف . أما دليلنا العقلي : فإن ميل القلب إلى جانب الضلال ، بدلاً عن جانب الهدى ، لا يمكن إلا لمرجح . وذلك المرجح ليس إلا الله . فوجب أن يكون ترجيح جانب الضلال. وهذا برهان قاطع لا يحتمل التأويل . فثبت : أن البرهان ليس إلا من جانبنا .
The response: As for his statement: 'The rational proofs indicated that it is not permissible for God to create disbelief in the servant,' we say: The rational proofs they mention amount to nothing more than the matter of praise and blame. And that is built upon rational good and evil. And we will show that this principle is extremely weak. As for our rational proof: The inclination of the heart towards misguidance, instead of guidance, is only possible due to a preponderator. And that preponderator is none other than God. So it is necessary that the preponderance of the side of misguidance occurs. And this is a decisive proof that does not admit of interpretation. So it is established that proof is only on our side.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that rational proofs indicate that God cannot create disbelief in a servant. The author responds by first pointing out that those rational proofs are built on the principle of rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyan), which he considers extremely weak. Then he presents his own rational proof: the inclination of the heart towards misguidance instead of guidance must have a preponderator (murajjih), and that preponderator can only be God. Therefore, it is necessary that the preponderance of the side of misguidance is from God. This is presented as a decisive proof that admits no interpretation. The author follows the order of the objection, then gives his response, first critiquing the opponent's basis, then providing his own proof, and concluding that the only valid proof is on his side. The passage guards against the fallacy of assuming that rational proofs based on human moral intuition are sufficient to limit God's actions.

Key Terms

الحسن والقبح العقليان
مرجح
برهان قاطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational proofs based on human moral intuition (such as rational good and evil) can limit God's actions.
Correction: The author argues that such proofs are extremely weak; the only valid rational proof shows God as the sole preponderator, meaning God's action is not constrained by human moral categories.

Checkpoint

B) The principle of divine omnipotence
C) The principle of prophetic infallibility
D) The principle of consensus

Key Takeaway
Only God can cause the inclination towards misguidance, and no rational proof based on human moral categories can constrain divine action.

The Two Meanings of Idlal (Misguidance)
Objection-and-answer: two types of idlal distinguished.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'mudill' (misleader) applies to both God and humans in two distinct senses—command versus creation.

Core Idea
Idlal has two meanings: commanding misguidance and directly creating it.

This passage tackles a potential contradiction: how can God call humans like Pharaoh and Iblis 'misleading' when only God can create misguidance? The author resolves this by showing that the word 'idlal' carries two different meanings—one by command and encouragement (applicable to humans), and another by direct causation (applicable to God). Understanding this distinction is crucial for a proper grasp of qadar and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument's structure: an objection followed by a response.
Notice the initial claim about Pharaoh, Iblis, and Samiri.
2
Identify the two types of idlal
Locate where the author divides idlal into two categories: by command/encouragement and by creating the motivating cause.
The Arabic keywords are 'بسبب الأمر به والترغيب فيه' and 'بسبب خلق الداعية الموجبة'.
3
Connect each type to the agent
Understand that the first type applies to humans (Pharaoh, Iblis) and the second to God. Note that the term 'mudill' is used uniformly but with different realities.
The commentary explains this distinction further.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Ask yourself how this distinction resolves the apparent conflict about misguidance and divine justice.
This shows that God's idlal is through creation of motives, while human idlal is through command, so no contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إنه تعالى حكم على فرعون وإبليس والسامري بكون كل واحد منهم مضلاً، مع أن أحداً منهم لا يقدر على خلق الضلال ) قلنا : إن الإضلال هو فعل ما يدعو إلى الضلال. سواء كان ذلك بسبب الأمر به ، والترغيب فيه . كإضلال فرعون وإبليس ، أو بسبب خلق الداعية الموجبة لذلك . كما في حق الله تعالى
As for his statement: 'God judged Pharaoh, Iblis, and the Samiri as misleading, even though none of them is able to create misguidance,' we say: Idlal is doing that which calls to misguidance. Whether that is by means of commanding it and encouraging it, like the misguidance of Pharaoh and Iblis, or by means of creating the motivating cause necessitating that, as in the case of God.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection points out that God called certain humans (Pharaoh, Iblis, and the Samiri) misleading (mudillun), yet they cannot create misguidance. The author responds by distinguishing two types of idlal: (1) idlal by commanding and encouraging (al-amr wa al-targhib), which applies to humans like Pharaoh and Iblis, and (2) idlal by creating the necessary motivating cause (khala al-da'iya al-mujiba), which applies to God. Thus, the term 'mudill' can be applied to both God and humans, but in different senses. The passage follows the objection, then provides a division of idlal into two categories, and clarifies that the human attribution is through command, not creation.

Key Terms
مضل
خلق الضلال
داعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Idlal only means directly creating misguidance, so if God calls humans 'misleading,' they must be able to create it.
Correction: Idlal also includes commanding and encouraging misguidance, so humans can be misleading without creating.

Checkpoint

A) They created misguidance in people
B) They commanded and encouraged misguidance
C) They were compelled by God
D) They were forced to misguide

Key Takeaway
Idlal is not limited to creation; humans can be misleading by commanding and encouraging misguidance, while God's idlal is through creating the inner impulse.

God's Preponderating Factor for Misguidance Can Be Counteracted
The author answers an objection about divine misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's action of idlal creates a preponderating factor that can be counteracted by other factors, so it does not necessarily cause actual misguidance.

Core Idea
God's idlal creates a preponderance, but other factors can oppose it.

This short passage responds to an objection about divine misguidance. The author defines idlal as an action that creates a preponderating factor toward misguidance. He then clarifies that this factor can be counteracted by other factors, explaining why divine idlal does not always result in actual misguidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the author's argument.
Notice the compressed style—every word matters.
2
Understand the objection being answered
The objection claims that if God causes someone to go astray, that person must go astray. But the author denies this implication.
3
Grasp the definition of idlal
Idlal is defined as doing that which necessitates the preponderance of misguidance—not directly causing it.
Key term: 'يقتضي ترجيح' (necessitates preponderance).
4
See how the necessitator can be opposed
The preponderating factor (al-muqtada) may be counteracted by something else, so it ceases to be effective.
This explains why divine idlal doesn't always lead to misguidance.
5
Reflect on the defense of divine justice
This argument clears God of causing actual misguidance by showing that other factors can oppose His preponderating factor.
This upholds human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إنه تعالى أضله فما أضل ) قلنا : المفهوم من الإضلال فعل ما يقتضي ترجيح جانب الضلال . ثم إن المقتضى قد يصير معارضاً بشيء آخر ، فيخرج عن كونه مقتضيا . وقد لا يصير كذلك
As for his statement: 'God made him go astray but he did not go astray,' we say: The understanding of idlal is doing that which necessitates the preponderance of the side of misguidance. Then the necessitator may become opposed by something else, so it ceases to be a necessitator. And it may not become so.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection raises the scenario where God is said to have misled someone, yet that person does not actually go astray. The author responds by defining idlal as an action that necessitates (yaqtadi) the preponderance of misguidance. However, this necessitator (al-muqtada) can be counteracted by something else, thereby ceasing to be effective. Conversely, it may not be counteracted. Thus, God's action of idlal provides a preponderating factor, but that factor is not always determinative; other factors can oppose it, explaining why not every instance of divine idlal results in actual misguidance. The passage follows the objection, defines idlal in terms of necessitating preponderance, and explains the possibility of opposition.

Key Terms
مقتضى
ترجيح
معارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's idlal is irresistible and directly causes misguidance.
Correction: The passage explains that idlal only necessitates a preponderance, which can be counteracted by other factors, so it does not force misguidance.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by saying that the necessitator (al-muqtada) may become opposed?
A) God's will is resisted by humans
B) The preponderating factor for misguidance may be counteracted by another factor
C) God sometimes fails to guide
D) Misguidance is not real

Key Takeaway
God's act of idlal creates a preponderating factor that can be counteracted, so it does not force misguidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself repeatedly drawn to a particular sin, do you ever blame God for making it easy or convenient? How does this lesson's distinction between a preponderating factor and a necessitator challenge that excuse, and what specific counteracting factor will you introduce this week to oppose that pull?
?
Think of a time you felt that God had 'misled' you because things didn't go your way. After reading this passage, can you identify a counteracting factor—something within your own effort or choice—that you neglected? What will you do differently next time you face a similar situation?
?
This lesson says God's idlal creates a preponderance but it can be counteracted. Is there an area of your life where you have been passive, assuming that God's decree makes change impossible? What is one concrete step you can take today to introduce a counteracting factor, trusting that your action matters?

Refuting the Objection: God's Creation of Disbelief
The author refutes an objection and presents his rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that God cannot create disbelief and establishes his own rational proof based on the necessity of a preponderating factor.

Core Idea
Rational proofs based on good and evil are weak; the decisive proof is that only God can cause the heart's inclination.

The author responds to an objection that rational proofs forbid God from creating disbelief. He dismisses those proofs as relying on the weak principle of rational good and evil. Then he presents his own airtight rational argument: the heart's turn toward misguidance must come from a preponderating factor, which can only be God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent claims rational proofs show God cannot create disbelief. This is based on the idea that some acts are inherently good or evil.
2
Examine the refutation
The author says those proofs rest on rational good/evil, which he will show is a weak foundation. Notice he does not deny all rational proofs, only this specific type.
3
Follow the decisive proof
The author argues that the heart's inclination toward misguidance requires a preponderating factor. Since only God can be that factor, the proof shows God creates disbelief.
This is presented as a decisive proof that admits no interpretation.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The author concludes that the only valid proof is from his side, meaning the Ash'ari position is rationally sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أما قوله : الدلائل العقلية ذلت على أنه لا يجوز أن يخلق الله الكفر في العبد ، قلنا : الدلائل العقلية التي يذكرونها لا تزيد على فعل المدح والذم . وذلك مبني على الحسن والقبح العقليين. وسنبين أن هذه القاعدة في غاية الضعف . أما دليلنا العقلي : فإن ميل القلب إلى جانب الضلال ، بدلاً عن جانب الهدى ، لا يمكن إلا لمرجح . وذلك المرجح ليس إلا الله . فوجب أن يكون ترجيح جانب الضلال. وهذا برهان قاطع لا يحتمل التأويل . فثبت : أن البرهان ليس إلا من جانبنا .
The answer: As for his statement: 'The rational proofs indicated that God cannot create disbelief in the servant,' we say: The rational proofs they mention are nothing more than [considerations of] praise and blame. And that is based on rational good and evil. We will show that this principle is extremely weak. As for our rational proof: The inclination of the heart towards the side of misguidance instead of the side of guidance cannot occur except through a preponderating factor. That preponderating factor is none other than God. So it is necessary that the preponderance of the side of misguidance [comes from Him]. This is a decisive proof that admits no interpretation. Thus it is established that the proof is only from our side.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection and includes a rational proof. The opponent claims that rational proofs show God cannot create disbelief in a servant. He then presents his own rational proof: the heart's inclination towards misguidance rather than guidance requires a preponderating factor (murjiḥ), and that factor can only be God. Therefore, God must cause the preponderance of misguidance. This proof is presented as decisive and unambiguous.

Key Takeaway

Two Kinds of Iḍlāl: Creatures vs. God
The author distinguishes iḍlāl by creatures from iḍlāl by God, responding to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between iḍlāl by creatures and iḍlāl by God, and how the term applies to both without requiring equal power.

Core Idea
Iḍlāl by creatures is commanding misguidance; by God, creating its compelling cause.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection: if God calls Pharaoh, Iblis, and the Samaritan 'those who lead astray,' but they cannot create misguidance, how is that possible? The answer lies in a nuanced definition of iḍlāl that distinguishes the means by which different agents cause misguidance. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection focuses on the label 'muḍill' being applied to creatures despite their inability to create misguidance.
The objection is a common one in qadar debates.
2
Identify the two types of iḍlāl
The author defines iḍlāl as 'doing that which calls to misguidance' and then gives two subtypes: by command/encouragement (creatures) and by creating the compelling cause (God). Find these in the text.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for debates about divine decree (qadar) and human responsibility.
This shows that the term 'iḍlāl' does not imply identical agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إنه تعالى حكم على فرعون وإبليس والسامري بكون كل واحد منهم مضلاً، مع أن أحداً منهم لا يقدر على خلق الضلال ) قلنا : إن الإضلال هو فعل ما يدعو إلى الضلال. سواء كان ذلك بسبب الأمر به ، والترغيب فيه . كإضلال فرعون وإبليس ، أو بسبب خلق الداعية الموجبة لذلك . كما في حق الله تعالى
His statement: 'Indeed, God Most High judged Pharaoh, Iblis, and the Samaritan as each being one who leads astray, even though none of them is able to create misguidance.' We say: iḍlāl is doing that which calls to misguidance, whether that is by commanding it and encouraging it, like the misguidance of Pharaoh and Iblis, or by creating the compelling cause for it, as is the case with God Most High.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage responds to the objection that God called Pharaoh, Iblis, and the Samaritan 'those who lead astray' (muḍillūn) even though they cannot create misguidance. The author argues that iḍlāl (causing misguidance) can be done in two ways: either by commanding and encouraging misguidance (as creatures do), or by creating the very cause that compels misguidance (as God does). So the term applies to both, without requiring equal power.

Correction: The passage clarifies that iḍlāl is defined as doing what calls to misguidance; creatures do it by command/encouragement, while God does it by creating the compelling cause.

D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
Iḍlāl by creatures is done by commanding misguidance, while iḍlāl by God is done by creating its compelling cause.
